PORTO ALEGRE


_To Luciana Mielniczuk (in memoriam)_


**ACKNOWLEDGMENTS**


On November 9, 2015, during the selection process for the master's program at PPGCOM, I

was nervous and frightened. I lived in the interior of the state, had visited Porto Alegre a few

times, and life in the capital was still something barely tangible. I had passed the written

examination at PPGCOM/UFRGS, and the time for the interview had come. My examining panel was


composed of two leading scholars in journalism and digital journalism research: Dr. Mar
cia Benetti and Dr. Luciana Mielniczuk. At a certain point in the conversation, when I thought

that my project on tobacco-related news coverage did not fit the research areas of either of them, I fell back on my last resort: I asked if I could

show the empirical _corpus_ I had collected. I had printed and organized the materials in a

folder made up of plastic envelopes, to make handling and presentation easier. The

following year, already enrolled in the master's program, Luti told me that this move had been decisive for her insight and her choice of me as her advisee: at that moment, when I

took the folder out of my backpack, she associated my materials with _databases_, and asked

whether I was interested in studying the subject. In that moment, a researcher in

Data-Driven Journalism was born. The tobacco-related coverage and the use of statistics

in that context, incidentally, was the first article I wrote during the master's program, in collaboration with

Luti and Francisco Amorim, now a Doctor of Sociology, an enthusiast of spreadsheets and quantitative

methods.

I thank Luti, to whom I dedicate this thesis, for seeing some potential in me; and for

being a visionary researcher and dedicated advisor. Beginning my academic career

with Luti provided me with the most diverse learnings, including teaching strategies she herself created, such as the "letter to Vítor" — a reference to Vítor Prado, at the time

an undergraduate research assistant, used to describe in detail the scientific method adopted

in the research — and the "ideas box," which served to ease my anxiety about

defining research parameters. Luti was like that: she had a solution for everything. Today, when I

find myself in a difficult situation, I try to imagine a dialogue with her.

I thank my advisor, Marcia Benetti, a leading figure in journalism studies, for her

warm welcome and the meaningful exchanges throughout the doctoral program, not only

in this thesis. An ethical, generous, empathetic, and inspiring professional, whom I look to as a model in

teaching, in research, and when I venture into writing chronicles. Marcia has

impeccable writing and a determined, resilient posture that I greatly admire. During the

Covid-19 pandemic, she spared no effort in preparing teaching and support materials for the students

of Fabico, taking into account their (limited) access to the internet and electronic

equipment. I was fortunate to begin and end this journey at PPGCOM with the precious

examining panel of 2015, composed of Luti and Marcia.

In this regard, I include my thanks to the Federal University of Rio Grande do

Sul (UFRGS) and to the Coordination for the Improvement of Higher Education Personnel (Capes),

respectively, for quality education and for the opportunity to pursue the master's and

doctoral programs on a full-time basis. I hope that the university and support for research will

withstand the dark times through which Brazilian science is passing.

I also thank the members of the examining committee, professors Marcelo Träsel, Josenildo

Guerra, Carlos Franciscato, and Suzana Barbosa, great names in journalism research

in Brazil, for their careful reading of this thesis. To the first two, for the dialogue during the defense

of the qualifying project, which was crucial to the direction of this research. To Professor Träsel, my

thanks for his presence, support, and dialogue throughout my entire period

as a graduate student at UFRGS. It was an honor to work so closely with my primary

reference in Data-Driven Journalism.

I am grateful to have met remarkable people during these six years, especially Luiza San
tos, Fabiana Freitas, Lucas Durr Missau, Ludimila Matos, Taís Seibt, Anelise Dias, and Samyr

Paz, and to have cultivated long-standing friendships (thank you, Érica Mallmann and Juliana Spilimbergo).

To my colleagues at the Journalism Research Center (Nupejor), I thank them for the Thursday

afternoons of readings and conversations, even at a distance. I extend my gratitude to the colleagues

of the Digital Journalism Research Group (JorDi) and to my students in the MBA in

Data Journalism at the Brazilian Institute for Education, Development, and Research (IDP),

where the Center for Data and Computational Journalism Studies (DataJor) was born.

To my parents, Marlise and Armênio, I thank them for building solid family foundations

through education and work; I recognize above all the role of my

mother in this context, and am grateful for the courage, determination, and strength she had to leave the interior

and pursue specialization at a time of few opportunities for women; to my sister,

Milena, for her support and the phone calls during difficult moments.

To my husband, Gabriel, my fortress, I am grateful for your understanding and companionship.

_Danke, Lieblingsmensch_ .


_"Without facts, you can't have truth. Without_

_truth, you can't have trust. Without trust, we_

_have no shared reality, no democracy, and it_
_becomes impossible to deal with our world's ex-_

_istential problems: climate, coronavirus, the_

_battle for truth."_


Maria Ressa
Nobel Peace Prize 2021


**ABSTRACT** [Portuguese]


This thesis investigates transparency in Data-Driven Journalism (DDJ), a body of
knowledge and practices linked to digital and networked journalism, situated within a scenario of
information overload and disinformation. It is grounded in theories of journalism
as a form (PARK, 1940; MEDITSCH, 1992; 1997) and forms (NIELSEN, 2017) of knowledge. Intertwined with fundamental values such as truth and objectivity, transparency (KARLSSON, 2010; 2020; 2022) concerns the openness of editorial processes and decisions,
allowing the audience access to information that is normally asymmetric in the relationship between the news outlet and the public. Thus, I present transparency in two
broad dimensions: as an **operator of ethical practice** and as a **discursive resource for**
**legitimation**. To examine how this value appears in journalistic products, I combine multiple case study methodology (STAKE, 2006) and the case study as illustration (MACHADO;
PALACIOS, 2006), observing the coverage of 11 reference and digital-native Brazilian newspapers in relation to the Covid-19 pandemic. As a result, I systematize 15 elements of transparency, distributed across **organizational** and **operational** levels, detected
in coverage produced within DDJ and presented here in alphabetical order: **byline, profile**
**and expertise**; **contact**; **error correction**; **publication date**; **documents**; **data**
**sources**; **funding sources**; **degrees of uncertainty**; **reporting markers**; **editorial**
**manual and principles**; **methodology**; **diversity policy**; **privacy**
**policy**; **content type**; **reproducibility and open-source code**. These parameters
serve as a guide for what I call the **Data-Driven Journalism Transparency**
**Protocol**, designed for the analysis of already-published journalistic products. Finally,
detecting a theoretical-experimental gap, I produce the proposal of a **Method for Data-**
**Driven Journalism with a focus on transparency and reproducibility** for
implementation in data-driven journalistic initiatives. I argue that the pandemic has accelerated the process of incorporating transparency as a fundamental value for

- DDJ, previously demonstrated in the discourse of professionals and now verified in the sphere of the product. The tendency is for these changes to endure and improve, reaffirming DDJ's commitment to approaching science via method in order to generate
a specific type of knowledge, more contextual and lasting.

**Keywords**: digital journalism; Data-Driven Journalism; Data Journalism;
transparency; Covid-19.


**ABSTRACT**


This doctoral dissertation investigates transparency in data journalism. Data journalism,
or data-driven journalism, is a set of practices and knowledge connected to digital and
network journalism. It is based on journalism theories that discuss the news as a form
(PARK, 1940; MEDITSCH, 1992; 1997) or forms (NIELSEN, 2017) of knowledge. Transparency (KARLSSON, 2010; 2020; 2022) is also connected to journalism fundamentals
such as truth and objectivity, so it comprehends openness of processes and editorial decisions, giving the audience access to information typically only used by journalists. In this
regard, I present transparency in two axes: as a **guide to ethical practices** and a **re-**
**source to legitimize news organizations**. To examine how this value appears in terms
of journalistic product, I combine the methodologies of multiple case studies (STAKE,
2006) and case studies as illustration (MACHADO; PALACIOS, 2006) to observe the news
coverage conducted by 11 news outlets (legacy media and native digital) during the
Covid-19 pandemic. As a result, I systematize 15 elements of transparency in two levels:
**organizational** and **operational**, here alphabetically organized: **byline, profile, and ex-**
**pertise**; **contact**; **content type**; **correction**; **data sources**; **diversity policy**; **docu-**
**ments**; **financing sources**; **manual of style and editorial principles**; **methodology**;
**open-source code and reproducibility**; **privacy policy**; **publication date; uncertainty**
**degrees**; **verification tactics**. These are the parameters for the **Data Journalism' Trans-**
**parency Protocol**, which is destined to analyze journalistic products that were already
published. In addition, observing a theoretical and experimental gap, I propose the **Data**
**Journalism Method focused on transparency and reproducibility** to be implemented
by news organizations in investigations using data. I argue that the pandemic has accelerated the presence of transparency as an essential value for data journalism. It was previously demonstrated by studies that included professionals' discourses, and now it is
possible to recognize it in the published content. The tendency is that those changes will
be maintained and improved, consolidating the commitment of data journalism to be
practiced towards science through the method and, thus, generate a specific type of
knowledge, which is contextual and has a longer duration.

**Keywords**: digital Journalism, Data-Driven Journalism, Data journalism, transparency,
Covid-19.

**APPENDIX A – STATE OF THE ART REVIEW ............................................................... 226**


**1 INTRODUCTION**


In March 2020, when the Covid-19 pandemic began to spread across the

world, open data activists immediately identified a problem: the precariousness

in the recording of cases and deaths from the disease. In Brazil, where information

is initially recorded at the municipal and state level, it falls to the Ministry

of Health to systematize and compile the numbers. For a series of reasons that will be explained

throughout the thesis, including the incompetence of the federal government, it became clear that

one could not count on the government for precise, localized records on the

pandemic, since it was releasing only coarsely granular information by state of the

federation.

The limitations in information and the absence of any prospect of improvement prompted

a partially independent count of Covid-19 cases and deaths —

partially because there was still a base of knowledge tied to the state

health agencies — by civil society initiatives, such as _Brasil.io_ [1],

still in March 2020; and by the consortium of Brazilian press outlets [2], formed in

early June of that year due to a "data blackout" registered at the Ministry

of Health. Other targeted initiatives, such as _Open Knowledge Brasil_ (OKBr), created ways

to monitor the quality of public information. OKBr, for example, founded the _Covid-19_

_Transparency Index_ [3], whose aim was to classify the level of openness of

state data and subsequently of capital city data.

In a country of continental dimensions like Brazil, where there is heterogeneity

between municipalities and regions, it was crucial from the outset that there be specific

and detailed data on the situation of the disease, so as to guide the government in

making conscious, evidence-based decisions. As could be seen in the country,

some mayors and governors took the situation seriously, suggesting containment

measures to slow the spread of the virus, such as social distancing and mask use. President

Jair Bolsonaro, in turn, chose to attack science and journalism, which he repeatedly

accused of spreading _fake news_, and opted to encourage the use of medications without

efficacy. As we found in a survey conducted at the beginning of the pandemic

1 Available at: https://brasil.io/covid19/
2 Available at: https://g1.globo.com/politica/noticia/2020/06/08/veiculos-de-comunicacao-formamparceria-para-dar-transparencia-a-dados-de-covid-19.ghtml
3 Available at: https://transparenciacovid19.ok.org.br/


(GEHRKE; BENETTI, 2021), politics and disinformation about Covid-19 are intimately

intertwined — of the corpus consisting of 407 units of false content

that we analyzed in that study, one quarter (25.5%) belonged to this field. Moreover,

a qualitative analysis of the materials showed that the president, his family members, and supporters

made efforts to shift blame onto mayors and governors for the negative economic

impact generated by the closure of commercial establishments to contain the

spread of the disease.

From the start of the pandemic, I was very close to the numbers related to

Covid-19. In addition to acting locally as a member of the Open Knowledge Brasil Ambassador Network

since 2019, organizing annual events such as Open Data Day,

I was one of the volunteers in the state of Rio Grande do Sul for _Brasil.io_. For six months,

two other colleagues and I collected daily the data on cases and deaths released

by the State Health Secretariat (SES/RS); to obtain the numbers at the national level,

around 40 volunteers worked daily throughout the pandemic. In the case of RS, we

even noticed advances in data openness: while the first bulletins were

released in closed file format (.pdf) and later in online format,

requiring scraping and organizing techniques, in the second half of 2020 it offered

the possibility of downloading spreadsheets in an open, non-proprietary

format (.csv). Until that point, one could say that the data were _available_, but not

_accessible_ because they were not offered in an open format that allowed for the reuse of

information and the production of new analyses. _Brasil.io_, in turn, was committed to

openness from the start, additionally explaining the collection methodology [4] in public

databases.

One of the first and most important lessons I drew from my experience with open

data and the Covid-19 pandemic was precisely the precariousness in the collection and opening

of information — in the first year, the problems were confined to case and death

numbers; in the second, the issues extended to vaccination records. As a

qualified mediator of reality, it would fall to journalism to explain to people what was

happening and how to interpret the whole situation. That is, journalists were required to

look skeptically at the statistics, understanding their contexts of production

and availability. More than explaining concepts like the moving average, which received

so much attention,

4 Available at: https://github.com/turicas/covid19-br/blob/master/api.md


putting the numbers in context required journalists to move closer to

science coverage. Science, in this case, in two senses: science communication, since

the pandemic is linked to the health field, and method, since journalists had to

understand the provisional nature of science and the cumulative character of this type of

knowledge, without there being a definitive and sudden answer to everything.

Faced with the provisional nature of the situation, I began to observe that, when drawing on

public databases to write reports, journalists needed, for example,

to expose the limitations of those records. They needed to explain that the numbers were not

detailed or that there could be delays in entering information into public

systems, which in turn would generate a temporary distortion in analyses. In some cases,

when certain statistics could not be used, data journalists would indicate,

in the stories, why they decided to exclude one or another state from the analysis,

or why such information should be observed with caution by the reader. They were,

then, explaining their working method, informing what sources were consulted,

how reliable those sources were, and how one or another result was reached.

In doing so, they were offering the public a kind of guide to reading their

work — put another way, they were offering **transparency**. Journalism, which so

often limits itself to demanding openness from the government, was finally

disclosing information — also of public interest — that had to do with

its method. It is likely that the pandemic accelerated the process of methodological

transparency in journalism, or that this happened specifically with Data-

Driven Journalism because, as we shall see, this set of practices

and knowledge is historically constituted on the basis of a scientific approach.

Given the complexity of the pandemic, which includes both information overload and

disinformation, there is a sense of content overload in the digital environment (BO
CZKOWSKI, 2021), which at times makes it difficult to know where trust should be placed. According to the _Digital News Report_ (NEWMAN _et al._ 2021), trust in news

rose three percentage points in Brazil in 2021, the second year of the pandemic, compared

to 2020. This means that 36% of people from a representative sample of the Brazilian

population declared trust in journalism — the highest index had been recorded in

2018, before the presidential elections, at 41%. It should be noted that during the presidential inauguration, in January 2019, supporters of Bolsonaro chanted "_Facebook_" and

"_WhatsApp_" [5] as a provocation to journalists covering the event on-site, as if the

platforms were neutral and receiving information gained more quality

without journalistic mediation.

Journalists and researchers, then, seek to look at transparency as a

fundamental value of journalism that can strengthen or even re-establish the link between

the public and the professionals of the field. Experienced and award-winning reporter at _Folha de_

_S.Paulo_, Patrícia Campos Mello (2020, n.p.) compares transparency to a journalistic version of "visit our kitchen," in which readers are invited to understand the ins and outs of production.

Karlsson (2022) identifies three waves of transparency in journalism, with the

current and third — beginning in 2016 — related to scrutiny and questioning

of established social institutions, which fits the scenario of disinformation and

public distrust. Thus, transparency in journalism would be a response to this

hesitation. The author, however, demonstrates a certain skepticism about the capacity of journalism

to open new information to readers, since the scenario of news production and distribution

is currently permeated by digital platforms and constant algorithmic

interference, making integral openness of a broader process unfeasible.

To address this issue, authors speak of algorithmic transparency

(DIAKOPOULOS, 2019), that is, the possibility of verifying computationally structured

information. As part of the scope of digital journalism, Data-Driven Journalism

presents characteristics that allow it to allocate human and technological resources toward transparency, such as the architecture of the Web, structured

through hyperlinks (MIELNICZUK, 2003). Thus, targeted solutions, such as the availability

of original sources, could be implemented without major technical difficulty.

In summary, transparency in journalism carries the meaning of openness,

of "strategically managed visibility" (KARLSSON, 2022, p. 78) and of reduction of information

asymmetry (KARLSSON, 2010; 2020; 2022), since the journalist has

first-hand access to data that the audience does not possess; thus, authors such as

Kovach and Rosenstiel (2011; 2014) advocate that ways be found for journalism to compensate for this

aspect, by revealing information about sources and reporting methods.

In Data-Driven Journalism, this includes, for example, providing hyperlinks to

5 Available at: https://noticias.uol.com.br/politica/ultimas-noticias/2019/01/01/para-reporter-daglobo-apoiadores-de-bolsonaro-gritam-whatsapp-e-facebook.htm


original databases and, when a programming language is employed, the source

code that drove the collection, analysis, and results. In this thesis, I argue that journalistic

products need to demonstrate not only operational aspects, but also

organizational ones, revealing funding arrangements and editorial policies that guide

decision-making, as illustrated in the iceberg metaphor (**Figure 7**).

This thesis presents elements of transparency found in informative content

of Data-Driven Journalism (DDJ). To do so, it draws on a theoretical foundation that understands

journalism as a form (PARK, 1940; MEDITSCH, 1992; 1997) or forms (NIELSEN,

2017) of knowledge, and DDJ as a body of knowledge and practices that approaches

science via method (GEHRKE, 2017; TRÄSEL; GEHRKE, 2021), the basic premise of

its oldest predecessor, Precision Journalism (MEYER, 2002).

My **hypothesis** is that, connected to truth and objectivity, transparency

becomes a fundamental value for the growing practice of Data-Driven Journalism,

which materializes in elements whose demonstration intensifies during periods of

significant events for humanity, such as the Covid-19 pandemic. Following from this hypothesis, my **main objective** is to identify and analyze the elements

of transparency activated in informative content (news stories, reports, and projects)

of DDJ published by Brazilian reference newspapers [6] and digital-native outlets [7]. The **specific objectives** are: a) to discuss Data-Driven Journalism as a form capable of generating

specific knowledge, linked to science; b) to understand how transparency

manifests in this type of journalism, relating it to journalistic ethics and

as a legitimation strategy; c) to systematize the transparency parameters verified

at the organizational level and at the operational level; and d) to develop a protocol for identifying transparency and a methodological proposal for implementation in DDJ. To test

my hypothesis and fulfill the objectives related to it, I employ a combination

of methodologies useful for the field of digital journalism and its innovative

characteristics: multiple case studies (STAKE, 2006) and the case study as illustration (MA
CHADO; PALACIOS, 2007).

6 The reference press consists of newspapers consolidated in the Brazilian scene for decades. In general,
it is composed of companies that initially published a print product, later migrating to the internet with a digital version — which occurred from 1995 onward. They are representative of a period
in which content was simply transferred from its print version to the online one (MIELNICZUK, 2003).
7 Digital natives are journalistic outlets whose emergence occurred on the Web, through the creation of a
news website, without prior experimentation in the print newspaper. As such, their language is already adapted for the use of hypertext. In Brazil, the creation of lasting enterprises in this segment intensified
from the 2010s onward.


Named as such in the first decade of the 2000s and practiced internationally

especially from 2010 onward, with the increase in public transparency policies and the

availability of databases, Data-Driven Journalism has received growing

attention in academia and in newsrooms (DE LIMA SANTOS, 2019), but exploration of

transparency in this type of journalism is limited to a few international works, such as

that of Zamith (2019), and a limited number of theses and dissertations in Brazil, which generally

discuss transparency from the perspective of legislation, as occurs through

the Freedom of Information Act (LAI) [8] and is demonstrated in the state-of-the-art review previously

presented in the thesis qualification (**APPENDIX A**).

This thesis is structured as follows: it presents an introduction, followed

by three theoretical chapters, a methodological chapter, and a contribution chapter, closing

with final considerations, references, and supplementary materials. Below,

I briefly present each of these parts, so that the introduction serves as a

guide for the reader.

In **chapter 2**, I discuss the contemporary scenario of information overload (BO
CZKOWSKI, 2021) and disinformation (WARDLE; DERAKHSHAN, 2017), intensified

by the Covid-19 pandemic and by denialist politicians such as Donald Trump, in the United

States, and Jair Bolsonaro, in Brazil, who accuse the press of fabricating "_fake news_."

As a qualified social practice that seeks to mediate between events

and the public, professional journalism must deal with this context, and one of the ways

of doing so is to offer traceable or replicable information, as occurs through practices

such as _fact-checking_ and Data-Driven Journalism. One of the most critical points

8 The Freedom of Information Act (LAI) — Law 12,527 — was enacted in May 2011 and has been in force since 2012 in
Brazil. It is inspired by similar laws around the world, such as the Freedom of Information Act (FOIA) in the
United States. The law regulates the right to information guaranteed by the Brazilian Federal Constitution of
1988 in its fifth article, reinforcing the publicity of information as the rule and secrecy as the exception.
Furthermore, it is structured in two dimensions: active and passive. In its active dimension, it determines that the
government at different branches (Executive, Legislative, and Judicial) and levels (federal, state, and municipal) must make public-interest information publicly available. In its passive dimension, it allows any
person to file a request to obtain information not yet released via the Citizen Information Service (SIC) — a tool available at the headquarters of the relevant public body — and digitally (e-SIC), through transparency portals. This occurs via the Citizen Information Service (SIC) — a tool normally
available in both physical and digital form (e-SIC) via transparency portals. When a request is
filed, there is a 20-day deadline for a response to the demand, a period extendable by ten more days.
If the release of information is denied, it is possible to appeal. Although it can be used by any
person, the passive dimension of the LAI is extensively used by Brazilian data journalists to
develop an original investigation on a topic.


under this aspect has to do with the distribution of news, which occurs mainly through

digital social networks and messaging apps, the same spaces through which

disinformation circulates.

In **chapter 3**, I situate Data-Driven Journalism in the modern context of the network

society (CASTELLS, 2016) and networked journalism (HEINRICH, 2011), as an integral

part of digital journalism (WAISBORD, 2019; ZELIZER, 2019) in a scenario of

transformation (DEUZE; WITSCHGE, 2020) and use of digital platforms (BELL;

OWEN, 2017). Given that the development of social practices is not uniform around the world,

I bring contributions from scholars on the development of the digital journalism field

in Brazil, such as Mielniczuk (2003) and Barbosa (2007). Aware that Data-Driven

Journalism is classified as the use of social knowledge associated with computing, from

data collection to visualization (TRÄSEL, 2014), I also highlight this

journalism's proximity to the scientific method, starting from the formulation of hypotheses, through

the search and analysis of data, and culminating in methodological openness through the lens of transparency.

**Chapter 4**, in turn, serves as a space for discussing what I consider three

guiding principles of journalism: truth, objectivity, and transparency, and how

these pillars are connected. I discuss the functional, provisional, and verifiable truth

(CORNU, 1994) characteristic of journalism and taken as the basis for practices that treat

objectivity in method (KOVACH; ROSENSTIEL, 2014; SPONHOLZ, 2010) as a

starting point, bringing the real fact (GUERRA, 2008) closer to the journalistic account (FRAN
CISCATO, 2005; LISBOA; BENETTI, 2015). Transparency (KARLSSON, 2010; 2020;
2022; MEIER, 2009), in turn, is associated with the openness of methods and editorial

decisions in journalism. For this reason, we classify and discuss transparency along two principal

dimensions: as an **operator of an ethical practice** and as a **discursive resource for**

**legitimation**. The prominent role of transparency among Brazilian data journalists (GEHRKE, 2020) and those of other nationalities (BEILER; IRMER;

BREDA, 2020) is also perceptible; for these professionals, this principle ensures greater proximity to the scientific

method and inspires trust and credibility with the audience, and is also capable of

combating disinformation.

To examine how the elements of transparency appear in Data-Driven Journalism based on

Brazilian newspapers' coverage of Covid-19, I dedicate **chap-**

**ter 5** to methodology, applying multiple case studies (STAKE, 2006) and the case study

as illustration (MACHADO; PALACIOS, 2007) to form a novel empirical corpus.

In doing so, I map 15 elements of transparency at the organizational and

operational levels by examining 11 Brazilian news outlets (reference and

digital-native), illustrating each of these findings.

The results that emerge from the corpus are fundamental for the elaboration of the **Trans-**

**parency Protocol in Data-Driven Journalism**, designed for the analysis of already published

journalistic products. This protocol originates in the mapping of elements

carried out in the methodological path, and brings together a series of questions that serve

as a guide for future research, in which transparency parameters can be evaluated

individually and even quantified. The Protocol is the first of two broad

contributions I make in **chapter 6** of the thesis, which culminates in the proposed **Method**

**for Data-Driven Journalism with a focus on transparency and reproducibil-**

**ity**, so that it can be implemented in data-driven journalistic initiatives.

Even aware of the limitations of transparency in journalism, I understand that the detection

and proposal of 15 elements in DDJ operate in consonance with what Karlsson (2022) indicates: so that the expectations of the public and the fulfillment of what that audience

expects to know are aligned, it is necessary that guides be established that can

be tested and followed. In the **final considerations** of this thesis, I seek to note that the work

serves as a foundation and starting point for new studies with the public, so

that one can understand what works and what does not work in specific niches when the

topic is transparency.

Transparency, I believe, becomes even more necessary and evident in moments

of crisis and major events, such as the Covid-19 pandemic. For this reason, it has the potential

to carry forward, and into other circumstances and scenarios, the lessons learned from this coverage,

which may serve as a watershed in the way journalism communicates

its method to the reader. The tendency is for these practices to be absorbed and incorporated

into daily life. In this regard, the construction of a protocol seeks to equip future researchers

who intend to monitor the progress of methodological and organizational

transparency in journalistic products, as well as to test new methods in their own work.


**2 INFORMATION AND DISINFORMATION IN COVID-19 COVERAGE**


The rapid geographic spread of the Sars-CoV-2 virus led the World Health Organization (WHO) to declare Covid-19 a pandemic on March 11,

2020. Since then, rumors, pseudoscientific data, and advice (WAISBORD, 2020) have circulated

particularly in the virtual environment, competing for space with reliable information

disseminated by scientific bodies and reproduced by the press. As symptomatic as

the fact that the WHO also described Covid-19 as a "massive infodemic" — an expression

adjusted to "disinfodemic" [9] by Posetti and Bontcheva (2020, p. 2) — is the nomination of the

International Fact-checking Network (IFCN) for the Nobel Peace Prize in recognition of its services

in combating disinformation during this period. Founded

in 2015, the IFCN's principal actions included an alliance among 99 outlets in 70

countries to monitor false content related to Covid-19 [10].

In this chapter, I bring elements that help comprehend the disinformation scenario

in which we are immersed, as well as discussing the conceptual dimensions of the terms

normally associated with the diffusion of false or low-quality content. Before

the deeper dive, I compile data on information consumption through digital social

networks. I also present a brief historical contextualization of the insidious use of science

to spread information contrary to its own principles. Although Covid-19

may seem like an unprecedented case, strategies to threaten the credibility of scholars and

scientific research have been recorded previously in history, particularly by

tobacco advocates, who questioned the relationship between smoking and the incidence of cancer.

Finally, I address Covid-19 as a journalistic event of our time — and which,

for this reason, has particularities in the way its coverage occurs — and I systematize the

9 The term infodemic (_infodemic_), according to the WHO, is related to the rapid proliferation of different types of
content about a topic of public interest, such as the pandemic. This scenario generates confusion and
risk behaviors, since the public has doubts about the reliability of the information. Subsequent to the WHO's use of the expression "massive infodemic," Posetti and Bontcheva (2020, p. 2) began to
use the term "disinfodemic" (_disinfodemic_), which combines infodemic and disinformation. For the authors,
disinformation related to Covid-19 is particularly dangerous because it has an immediate impact
on all people, being more toxic and lethal than other topics. Although the WHO used
_infodemic_ as a metaphor to summarize the rapid spread of pandemic-related disinformation, authors such as Simon and Camargo (2021) criticize the uncritical use of the term. Among the justifications,
the authors argue that information does not spread in the same way as a virus, generating the risk of
oversimplifying this process and the way disinformation is actually generated and spread. Thus, they say
that easily adopted metaphors have the potential to cause more confusion, and are not always useful for
describing a phenomenon.
10 Available at: https://piaui.folha.uol.com.br/lupa/2021/01/21/ifcn-nobel-da-paz/


main limitations and challenges of this era, which presents _fact-checking_ and its principle

of methodological transparency, similar to what occurs in Data-Driven Journalism,

as one of journalism's principal responses to disinformation.

Throughout the thesis, I use the term **disinformation** (GEHRKE; BENETTI, 2021; PO
SETTI; BONTCHEVA, 2020; TANDOC JR; LIM; LING, 2018; WARDLE; DERAKHSHAN,

2017) to refer to a type of informational fraud that distorts reality or removes

it from context. In general, this type of misleading content is created and distributed to

damage the reputation of people and institutions, and is commonly motivated by ideological

and economic concerns (TANDOC JR; LIM; LING, 2018). It can be disseminated through

different formats (text, audio, photo, video, and others) and circulates primarily through

the internet, especially through digital social networks and messaging

apps.

As we shall see below, this phenomenon takes shape with a type of false content —

the so-called _"fake news"_ — which can be considered a genre within the broad scope

of disinformation, and is frequently associated with the political sphere (DOURADO, 2020).

Since news produced in journalism is anchored in a factual reality,

the term _fake news_ becomes an oxymoron — strictly speaking, there are no _false news_ — incapable of

describing a broad and complex phenomenon such as disinformation. Added to this is the

hollowing out of the term, which in addition to being ammunition to question the credibility of the press

has become synonymous with lying (EGELHOFER _et al._, 2020) and has come to be adopted in

any everyday circumstances. I also emphasize that disinformation is different from journalistic error

(unintentional, it is presumed) and from low-quality journalism, which includes

the use of unclear reporting methods and uncritical reproduction of official

discourses. Journalism is expected to demonstrate a commitment to truth (KOVACH; ROSENSTIEL,

2014) and to qualified (REGINATO, 2019) and contextualized [11] information (LÜCKMAN,

2020).

In the past, informational noise caused rumors to spread about the

supposed existence of foods shaped like animals. In the Middle Ages, the travel accounts

of the knight Sir John Mandeville surprised readers with their novelty —

11 I draw on the definition of Lückman (2020, p. 193), for whom contextualization "[...] seeks to situate
the journalistic event within the section of reality to which it belongs, establishing the
greatest possible number of connections between that event and the relevant elements associated with it,
starting from the singular aspects and identifying related, pertinent, and consistent information that
contributes to broadening the critical understanding of the topic, thus enabling the production of knowledge."


which later proved to be unfeasible: upon passing through India, he supposedly encountered

trees whose fruits resembled small lambs, as if they were full

of flesh and blood. Known as the Vegetable Lamb of Tartary, this type of plant-animal

(which in fact never existed) was not the only one to spread through the Middle Ages, nor

the only one to be accepted as true even by botanists and other scholars until it was

debunked in the seventeenth century (O'CONNOR; WEATHERALL, 2019).

One of the reasons the myth that animals grew on trees lasted

four centuries was the trust attributed to the people who spread it. After all, it seemed

rather obvious to believe a traveler who observed everything with his own eyes and recorded

his notes. For O'Connor and Weatherall (2019), what explains people's adherence to

the lamb rumor has nothing to do with botany or the natural world, but is purely

a social phenomenon related to the way beliefs are acquired and spread. Situations like this raise questions about how beliefs are formed, spread, and even

maintained without scientific evidence, and that this does not necessarily depend on

technology, even though technology has the potential to expand the channels of dissemination.

Although it seems absurd today, adherence to the vegetable lamb is not very

different from the case of the _boimate_ recorded in Brazil in the 1980s, with the additional

aggravation that the error was propagated even by the press, albeit unintentionally. At

the end of April 1983, the magazine _Veja_ published a piece entitled "Fruto da carne" [Fruit of Flesh], in which

it announced the supposed discovery of two German biologists who had supposedly fused the cells of

a cow with those of a tomato plant, creating a hybrid product, the _boimate_ — a fruit that

would taste like steak with tomato. The report in the Brazilian magazine was based on a

text published by _New Scientist_ as an April Fools' joke — doing such pranks in

reference to April Fools' Day was a tradition in the British press, but it was taken as

true by the _Veja_ team. Almost a year later, the magazine published a correction.

In the current Brazilian context, disinformation is part of a broad political scenario that

includes the rise of Jair Bolsonaro to the presidency. In October 2018, _Folha de_

_S.Paulo_ revealed that pro-Bolsonaro businesspeople were planning to finance, one week before

the second round of elections, a massive messaging campaign via _What-_

_sApp_ [12] to attack his political opponents and benefit the then-candidate. This was

an irregular action because, in the country, corporate campaign financing is not permitted,

only that of natural persons.

12 Available at: https://www1.folha.uol.com.br/poder/2018/10/empresarios-bancam-campanha-contra-o-pt-pelo-whatsapp.shtml


Following the publication, journalist Patrícia Campos Mello,

author of the report in _Folha_, became the target of misogynistic attacks and threats to her

integrity and that of her family. The details of this case were narrated by Mello (2020) in

the book _A Máquina do Ódio_ [The Hate Machine].

Dourado's (2020) thesis also indicates that Bolsonaro was the main beneficiary

of disinformation narratives in the 2018 presidential elections, since the majority

of fraudulent messages displayed an anti-PT and pro-Bolsonaro sentiment. To reach this conclusion, the author initially mapped 346 texts classified

as false by five fact-checking projects, selecting 57 for deeper analysis. Within

this sample, she found that 1,073 accounts on social networks led the process of

spreading disinformation, which reached 4 million shares in the months

before the 2018 elections. Among the research findings, she noted that humans,

not robots from automated accounts, are among the main propagators of false content,

and that such content moves organically through social network sites and other platforms,

primarily _WhatsApp_ and _Facebook_.

In a different but analogous context, a similar situation was diagnosed in our own study (GEHRKE; BENETTI, 2021), which analyzed 407 texts related to Covid-19 and classified

as false by fact-checking agencies. This material circulated through different spaces

simultaneously, and we therefore observed a total of 493 occurrences on social network

platforms. More than half (65.11%) of these episodes occurred on _Facebook_, while

_WhatsApp_ appears next, with 15.82% of cases. Even with the caveat that

there is a project between technology companies and fact-checking agencies for

content verification, _Facebook_ stands out as one of the spaces through which a

significant portion of existing disinformation circulates.

Also along these lines, a study by Rossini _et al._ (2020) using _Facebook_ and

_WhatsApp_ suggests that people most engaged in political discussions tend to

share disinformation more frequently, even if accidentally. In the research,

it was found that the risk of sharing false content unintentionally is minimized by

education level — the higher the level of schooling, the lower the propensity.

It is also _Facebook_ and _WhatsApp_ that are the greatest allies of Brazilians in obtaining

information, according to _Digital News Report_ data (NEWMAN _et al._, 2021). The social

network has 141.4 million users in Brazil and is used as a source by 47% of Brazilians. The messaging app, in turn, is part of the routine of 120 million

people in the country, serving as a source for 43% of the population. Furthermore, the report indicated

that the mobile phone is the device most used for seeking information, cited

by 77% of respondents. The computer comes next, declared

by 36% of people as their access device.

The significant adoption of mobile phones in Brazilian consumer habits presents

a particularly notable situation in Brazil: mobile internet. One of the factors that

may contribute to the high search for news on digital social networks and messaging apps

is the fact that mobile carriers typically sell packages with free access to social network apps, meaning they are not included in internet data plans. As a result, there are fewer financial costs

regardless of the time spent on digital platforms.

Added to this is the discredit attributed by public figures, such as the Brazilian

president, to the press. Far-right politicians like him — and Donald Trump [13] in the United

States, until 2020 — encourage people to distance themselves from professional journalism,

which they mistakenly call _fake news_, and to consume content only

on the platforms, where they maintain control over the content disseminated and commonly

fire off lies to inflame their most loyal followers. In doing so, they deflect from the role of

qualified mediator that the press must perform. The presidential inauguration of

Jair Bolsonaro on January 1, 2019 was a significant indicator. On that date,

supporters of the president gathered in the Praça dos Três Poderes [Square of the Three Powers] in Brasília and, provoking

journalists from Rede Globo, chanted "_WhatsApp_" and "_Facebook_" [14]. The verbal attacks

by Bolsonaro on the professional press are also a case apart: in 2020,

the National Federation of Journalists (Fenaj) tallied 299 occurrences [15].

In addition to _Facebook_ and _WhatsApp_, researchers have in their sights _YouTube_,

_Instagram_, and _Twitter_, which account, respectively, for 39%, 30%, and 12% of

Brazilians' preferences in online information consumption (NEWMAN _et al._, 2021). Studies

such as that of Machado _et al._ (2020a) show that _YouTube_ has been becoming a fertile

environment for the spread of disinformation. With approximately 120 million

13 In January 2021, Twitter banned Donald Trump's account following unfounded allegations of fraud
in the 2020 American elections, which culminated in Joe Biden's victory, and after he incited the invasion of the Capitol.
The social network determined that the posture of the then-president violated community rules. The full
statement is available at: https://blog.twitter.com/en_us/topics/company/2020/suspension.html
[14 Available at: https://noticias.uol.com.br/politica/ultimas-noticias/2019/01/01/para-reporter-da-](https://noticias.uol.com.br/politica/ultimas-noticias/2019/01/01/para-reporter-da-globo-apoiadores-de-bolsonaro-gritam-whatsapp-e-facebook.htm)
[globo-apoiadores-de-bolsonaro-gritam-whatsapp-e-facebook.htm](https://noticias.uol.com.br/politica/ultimas-noticias/2019/01/01/para-reporter-da-globo-apoiadores-de-bolsonaro-gritam-whatsapp-e-facebook.htm)
15 Available at: https://fenaj.org.br/nove-meses-bolsonaro-299-ataques/


users in Brazil, around 70% of the platform's traffic results from recommendations.

In this way, algorithms operate according to users' preferences, suggesting other videos

for consumption. Empoli (2020) reports that in 2015, conspiracy videos spread on

_YouTube_ at the time of the Zika virus outbreak, hampering doctors

who were trying to distribute vaccines and larvicides to control the mosquitoes responsible

for transmission. Research by Massarani _et al._ (2021) with young Brazilians aged 15 to 24

years shows that _YouTube_ is being used as a search engine

almost as much as _Google_: 73% and 79% of respondents, respectively, indicated

its use.

Journalism's dependence on digital social networks for content distribution

is one of the points discussed by Bell and Owen (2017). In this environment, it is the

platforms that set the rules of the game and condition the press to follow certain

premises in order to expand its reach. Thus, the use of sensationalist headlines

by journalism to secure more clicks — the so-called _clickbaits_ — is notable.

However, this is a dangerous strategy because, in the digital environment, people

are liable to consume news accidentally or simply because they skim quickly over headlines,

engaging in a process of scanning (COSTERA

MEIJER; GROOT KORMELINK, 2015), eliminating any deeper engagement. It becomes

a challenge for journalism to operate in a scenario where large technology companies

play a prominent role, displacing the role of the journalist and of journalism itself in

an ecosystem of information production and circulation (ANDERSON; BELL; SHIRKY,

2013), without this necessarily being something beneficial for society as a whole.

User data tracking is one of the primary means of monetization

for the platforms, since the data collected are used for targeted advertising.

Thus, these companies assume broader control of social and cultural networks,

also generating implications for digital journalism, according to Steensen and Westlund

(2021). The authors map three main changes: a massive crisis in business

models, since advertisers have migrated to digital platforms; an emphasis on

the use of audience data and site traffic metrics; and changes in news distribution patterns,

a space that non-journalistic companies have come to dominate. The impact

of these variables on the journalistic field can be perceived in the context of area studies:

technology and platforms were the principal topics related to digital journalism

studies in 343 publications of the journal _Digital Journalism_ from 2013 to 2019.

According to research by Rossini and Kalogeropoulos (2021), the majority of Brazilians

sought information about the Covid-19 pandemic on television (82%), on digital social

networks such as _Facebook_ and _Twitter_ (81%), and on _WhatsApp_ (81%). Notably,

in this study, one quarter of respondents said they actively avoided news about

Covid-19. This attitude is more common among people who identified politically as right-wing

(44%) and occurs in smaller proportions among those who identify as center (33%) and

left-wing (23%). Among the main reasons for the deliberate avoidance of news

is the excessive repetition by the press (87%), the negative effect on people's mood

(66%), the lack of trust in news (57%), and the sense of powerlessness in the face

of events (53%). Although it is difficult to simplistically correlate any variables

associated with disinformation, it is symptomatic that the right-wing public identified

with the president — who, in turn, attacks the press — avoids consuming news.

Particularly since the 2010s, with the rise of social networks, the information scenario

became complex with the inclusion of new actors (WAISBORD,

2020), including those who bring chaos and seek to undermine citizens' trust in science.

We shall see below that this is not the first time a case of this nature has occurred, and that

disinformation is not new, but has channels through which it can spread rapidly — beyond

organic interactions, we are talking about the actions of _bots_ and _trolls_ in the disputes over

truth (KAKUTANI, 2018). The role of political actors is pivotal for understanding the

dynamics involving disinformation on digital social networks.


**2.1 The complex scenario of disinformation and its variations**


Around the time of the US presidential race, on December 4, 2016, Edgar Maddison Welch left his hometown, Salisbury, and drove to Washington,

D.C. to verify, with his own eyes, the location attributed to a supposed pedophilia network connected to candidate Hillary Clinton. With the intention of saving the children who

supposedly were victims of abuse at the site — and who were never found — he opened

fire on the pizzeria _Comet Ping Pong_. Employees managed to flee and no one was

injured. Interviewed [16] by a journalist from _The New York Times_ after surrendering to police,

Welch, the father of two girls, said that, from reading a series of articles

and a "combination of sources," he had found evidence that something nefarious was occurring at the

pizzeria and decided to go to the location to check for himself. A few days before, he had installed an internet

service at his home.

The chain of disinformation that led Welch to take a drastic action

is known as _Pizzagate_, a conspiracy theory created from the

email correspondence of the pizzeria owner regarding a fundraising dinner for

Clinton's campaign. These messages, from campaign chairman John Podesta,

were leaked by _WikiLeaks_ [17] and, at some point, people began to associate the use of

terms such as _cheese pizza_ with _child pornography_.

The _Pizzagate_ case illustrates with precision the chaotic scenario of today, in which

diverse types of information and disinformation coexist and can be accessed through

search engines and digital social networks. Conspiracy theories, also present

in this environment, add a dangerous ingredient to this scenario. Like

other types of disinformation, they draw on themes that provoke outrage and indignation.

Welch declared having been motivated by the impulse to defend children.

The 2016 American elections were marked not only by this case

but also by Donald Trump's recurrent use of the term "_fake news_" to

attack the credibility of the press. After him, other far-right politicians began

to adopt similar discourses and strategies. Research shows that false content

generally has greater propagation potential than truthful content. In a recent scenario,

16 Available at: https://www.nytimes.com/2016/12/07/us/edgar-welch-comet-pizza-fake-news.html
17 An organization coordinated by journalist and activist Julian Assange, _WikiLeaks_ is a platform that publishes
information and documents leaked by anonymous sources, generally on sensitive topics.


O'Connor and Weatherall (2019) mention that, at the time of Trump's election in 2016, the

20 most widely circulating pieces of disinformation received 8.7 million likes and shares

in the three months before the elections. News shared by reliable sources,

in turn, reached a mark of 7.3 million likes or shares on Face
book. It appears that falsehood has a compelling narrative and is capable of generating engagement.

For Kakutani (2018, p. 11), Trump's rise to power represents the triumph of the

assault on reason and truth, but the process began to develop earlier:

it is the result of a sentiment incubated by the far-right of the United States

for years.

With Trump, the personal is political and, in many respects, he
is less a caricature anomaly and more a bizarre epitome of
a series of broader, interrelated attitudes that slowly corrode
truth today, from the blending of news and politics with entertainment to the toxic polarization
that has taken hold of American politics, passing through
the growing populist contempt for expertise.


Regardless, Trump's candidacy only prospered because, as the

author examines, there exists a posture of indifference among people toward facts and

factual truth. Or, at worst, figures like him and other populist politicians are

seen as someone who tells the truth, as also observed by Empoli (2020).

Rumors, falsehoods, and manipulations have been recorded at different moments in

history, as seen in the Middle Ages through the Vegetable Lamb of Tartary.

The new and dangerous ingredient is the high propagation potential and the speed with which this

occurs through digital social networks and messaging apps. The digital environment

also contributes to conceptual confusion about sources of information, since receiving

content from people one is close to can help legitimize the veracity of information

that is being shared (TANDOC; LIM; LING, 2018).

As stated earlier, in this thesis I use the term **disinformation** to designate

misleading content that circulates with the purpose of deceiving and undermining reputations. Among

other factors relating to the association of journalism with the falsehoods circulating on the Web,

I agree with Wardle and Derakhshan (2017), for whom the term _fake news_ is essentially

too shallow to describe a complex phenomenon that encompasses information pollution.

Thus, the authors hold that three terms can describe more precisely what

occurs in this scenario. They are: _misinformation_ — when false content is shared

without the intention to deceive; _disinformation_ — when content circulates to cause

harm; _malinformation_ — when truthful information is shared to

cause harm, normally associated with something that should have remained in the private

sphere. In broad, generic terms, all three can be rendered as "desinformação" in Portuguese.

Commonly invoked by politicians, the term _fake news_ can be understood as

a subtype of informational fraud that seeks to mimic news (TANDOC JR;

LIM; LING, 2018). Dourado (2020, p. 54) defines it as "[...] a type of false or distorted information that simulates a news item — or novelty — in order to narrate political facts and

thus gain greater visibility in transit across social media platforms." In

her view, the urgent character of journalism is fundamental in this case, since the

so-called _fake news_ mimics the idea of novelty and alleged public interest. Thus,

disinformation mimics the format of news — often including supposed

sources — making use of headlines, photos, and texts. This type of content also commonly presents

phrases like "urgent" and "share with your contacts." The process

of fabricating disinformation makes the intent to deceive even more evident. These are signs

of this deliberate confusion, according to Dourado (2020): the use of _clickbait_ in headlines, video

or image montages, and other graphic pieces.

Authors such as Egelhofer _et al._ (2020) observe a hollowing out of meaning

in relation to the term _fake news_. It is notable that the term is being used excessively, normally

referring to lies and inaccuracies in general, which occurs, including, in

publications released by the press itself. For the researchers, one of the problems with the term

circulating freely within journalism itself is its use by political actors, who feel

at ease to employ it in attacking the legitimacy and credibility of the press

as a democratic institution.

If for theorists the term _fake news_ can identify a type of disinformation,

among most people there are no clear signals. Through questionnaires and focus

groups, Nielsen and Graves (2017) observed that the public notably associates

low-quality journalism (sensationalism, for example), propaganda, and some types

of advertising with the term _fake news_, even though, at first, this type of

content is not produced with the intent to disinform. Thus, the researchers observed

that the public associates the term _fake news_ not only with false content, but also

with a broader (dis)informational [18] scenario that includes the press, politicians, and

the platforms.

There are also other subdivisions within the breadth of disinformation or _fake_

_news_. Broadly speaking, Wardle and Derakhshan (2017) and Wardle (2019) classify disinformation into: fabricated content, manipulated content, imposter content, false context, misleading content, false connection, and satire or parody. Tandoc Jr., Lim and Ling (2018),

in turn, observe the following typology: satire, parody, fabrication, manipulation, advertising, and propaganda. Fabrication, the authors state, is what could most

closely be classified as what is known as _fake news_.

Regarding the type of content most widely circulated during the pandemic, we identified

(GEHRKE; BENETTI, 2021) that, of the 300 qualitatively analyzed cases, 145 (48.43%)

employ false context, that is, they make use of a true fact or genuine image, but

remove the materials from context to create a falsehood. The second most commonly

invoked content type is fabricated content, with 86 (28.67%) occurrences. This is content created

to deceive, including the use of research institutions and fictitious names in an attempt to

attribute credibility to what was written. The results align with what was detected

by Dourado (2020), who observed that the use of photos and videos functions as a

sort of guarantee or evidence that the story being told is plausible.

Covid-19 is not the first scientific event to be linked to

disinformation. As observed by Machado _et al._ (2020b), there is not necessarily

a questioning of science or its methods, but similar strategies

are used to spread disinformation, such as uncertainty, which is inherent to science as

there is a systematic production of knowledge and the validity of theories

until they are replaced by others that better account for reality.

In the second half of the twentieth century, still without the major influence of digital

technology as we know it today in the distribution of disinformation, the tobacco sector discovered

in the uncertainty inherent to the scientific process a possibility to exploit and

18 To dissociate the term _fake news_ from what is expected of trustworthy journalism, Träsel (2019, n.p.) proposes
the use of the term "pseudojournalism" to designate "texts, audios, images, or videos that mimic journalistic
formats in an attempt to deceive the public," that is, relating to falsified content.
The prefix _pseudo_ corresponds to the artificiality of this type of content, and the term journalism, in Portuguese,
also encompasses formats that, strictly speaking, are not only news (but also reports and other formats). In English, the term _news_ is broader and can be used to refer to other journalistic formats. Although the author's argument makes sense, I view the use of pseudojournalism with reservations due to
the use of "journalism" in the formation of the word, which could reinforce pre-existing beliefs that
journalism does not operate on the basis of factual truth.


weaken the claim that smoking causes cancer. As Oreskes and Conway

(2010) explain, the aim was to exploit the gaps that naturally exist in science to

draw attention and raise questions about the risks caused by cigarettes. From the 1950s onward,

research confirmed the relationship between tobacco use and the development of

cancer. However, the tobacco industry chose to sponsor studies that explored other types of relationships without considering cigarettes, associating cancer with heredity,

for example. In doing so, it sought to perpetuate the idea that there were not enough studies to

show the relationship between smoking and developing cancer.

Naturally, to spread these ideas, tobacco industry businesspeople took

advantage of the fact that the press had to show both sides. "The industry's position was

that there was 'no proof' that tobacco was bad, and they fostered that position by

manufacturing a 'debate', convincing the mass media that responsible journalism

had an obligation to present 'both sides' of it" [19] (ORESKES; CONWAY, 2010, p. 16). The tobacco chain made good use of the strategy that journalism

should report the facts in a balanced way and, therefore, make space for them to present

their side of the story. The authors reflect that, in this case, there were not two sides to

show; balance was interpreted as providing equal weight to both sides, rather

than offering a fair weight to both sides.

Like any other social network formed by human beings, scientists

are also influenced by social factors and by their peers, since they are part of epistemic communities, meaning groups of people who are trying to gain knowledge about

the world in some way. To understand how disinformation persists, it is necessary

to look at how social interactions have changed over time, and how this

plays out in the formation of beliefs.

Considering the context of digital social networks, people and algorithms — computational instructions aimed at achieving a particular objective — intermingle. Algorithms

are written by humans, and social network sites do not clearly indicate what types

of instructions they prioritize in interactions. As content sharing occurs

on the platforms, such information gains relevance and becomes more visible,

19 Original: "The industry position was there was 'no proof' that tobacco was bad, and they fostered that
position by manufacturing a 'debate', convincing the mass media that responsible journalism had an obligation to present 'both sides' of it."


especially when it circulates through the accounts of influencers and political actors. As

we shall see below, some people play a key role in network interactions.


**2.2 Top-down: the influence of politicians and public figures**


Throughout 2020, according to data from the fact-checking agency _Aos Fatos_ [21], President

Jair Bolsonaro made 915 false or imprecise statements about the Covid-19

pandemic. In his memoirs, former Health Minister Luiz Henrique Mandetta, who was

in office when the disease began to spread through Brazil from January 25 onward, said that, still in early March, the Palácio do Planalto was operating as if the new coronavirus did not exist. According to Mandetta (2020), Bolsonaro never agreed to analyze charts

and confront the reality of the disease that the government would need to face. This was reflected

in the president's actions: throughout the pandemic, he minimized the virus, criticized social

distancing, and encouraged people to take ineffective medications.

Political actors play a fundamental role in networks and, therefore, in the propagation

of information or disinformation. Although there are strategies and mechanisms of propagation, studies suggest that an individual's influence can be decisive

in the spread of false content. In the research by Machado _et al._ (2020a), carried out

through network analysis and lexical analysis, people with some degree of social authority, even those distant from science, drew on their hierarchical positions within

their sphere of action to challenge the guidelines proposed by expert systems such as

the press, universities, and international organizations, thereby supporting conspiracy

theories.

In her thesis, Dourado (2020) found that personal profiles are the most relevant

in the propagation of disinformation, showing that this phenomenon spreads far more

through organic than automated channels. To support this, the study showed the prevalence

of personal profiles among the principal disseminators in general and among those coded

as the most relevant propagators. At the same time, she also discovered

that, among the most relevant personal profiles, those that did not appear to be bots (that is,

automated accounts) were nearly five times more frequent than those that

appeared to be bots. This means that, however much the climate of informational fraud is

21 Available at: https://www.aosfatos.org/todas-as-declara%C3%A7%C3%B5es-de-bolsonaro/


inflamed by the use of bots, _trolls_ (people who sow doubt to generate hate speech
or make provocations and spread lies for entertainment) and fake profiles, the distribution

of _fake news_ about the 2018 elections in Brazil happened through far more organic

than automated or artificial channels.

Wardle and Derakhshan (2017) propose examining the elements of disinformation

separately, namely: the agent, the message, and the interpreters. The agent,

for example, can operate in different instances, such as fabrication and distribution. And this is precisely

one of the main challenges in disinformation studies: understanding what

the motivations of people are. As we observed in our research (GEHRKE; BENETTI,

2021), it is not possible to assert that the propagators of disinformation are also the creators

of the rumors; but it is a fact that these people have a high degree of influence in

networks. Regarding Covid-19, federal deputy Osmar Terra and members of the Bolsonaro

family, including the president himself, are among those who most spread disinformation,

according to our survey.

In research examining disinformation about Covid-19 on _YouTube_, Ma
chado _et al._ (2020a) identified two main narratives related to the disease: one

of a geopolitical character, blaming China for the supposed production of the virus as a biological weapon and using it for economic purposes; and another of a religious character, seeking to frame

the pandemic within a context of biblical symbolism. In the case of the religious narrative,

one of the prominent figures in the spread of disinformation is pastor Silas Mala
faia, leader of the neopentecostal church Assembleia de Deus and a supporter of President Jair Bolsonaro. In this narrative, data and scientific aspects are distortedly used to

associate the pandemic with a divine plague. Another person who appeared in this research as

a significant agent in the diffusion of disinformation was journalist Alexandre Garcia, former

host at Rede Globo. In one of the videos published on _YouTube_, he questions the

circulation and persistence of the virus in tropical environments like Brazil. The common thread

among the campaigns identified by Machado _et al._ (2020a) was the minimization of the

pandemic. The authors assess that, when convenient, the discourse of disinformation distorts

science and decontextualizes points from technical notes.

Other studies (MACHADO _et al._, 2020b; SOARES; RECUERO, 2020) have also demonstrated

the influence of figures like Bolsonaro in the Brazilian disinformation scenario. By analyzing a dataset composed of 8,600 posts verified

by 70 fact-checking agencies in 134 countries, Machado _et al._ (2020b) found that

disinformation about Covid-19 follows regional patterns, involving cultural,

geographic, and political factors. In this scenario, countries like Brazil and India find

themselves more isolated than the rest of the world. In Brazil's case, the main reason is Bolsonaro's insistence on the use of measures without scientific proof, such as the drugs

hydroxychloroquine, azithromycin, and ivermectin.

In his memoirs, Mandetta (2020, p. 133) clarifies Bolsonaro's insistence

on chloroquine: for the president, it would be enough to offer a remedy for workers

to return to activity and to get the economy moving. "For Bolsonaro the solution was

always simple: his project for combating the pandemic was to say that the government has the

medicine and that whoever takes the medicine will be fine. Only those who were going to die

anyway will die."

Research such as that of Rossini and Kalogeropoulos (2021) suggests that trust in the

federal government is higher among Brazilians who have right-wing political preferences,

that is, those who are aligned with the government. In general, respondents said they trust

scientists most for obtaining information about the pandemic — percentages varied

from 70% (right-wing) to 90% (left-wing). In second place, participants said they trust

the information provided by the World Health Organization (WHO), reaching a

percentage of 86% among left-wing people, with the exception of right-wing participants,

who say they trust the Ministry of Health more (63%) and the federal government (63%).

We have seen, in this section, that political actors and influencers play an important

role in the diffusion of information or disinformation, given that they play a decisive role

in the way networks are organized and because of their reach in the network.

Below, we shall examine what the main measures are to combat disinformation.


**2.3 The effectiveness of disinformation and strategies to combat it**


Researchers from different areas of knowledge seek strategies to combat

disinformation. In this scenario, one of the greatest challenges is understanding the

intentions that motivate the agent creating or disseminating false content, a practice generally

motivated by ideological or economic concerns (TANDOC JR; LIM; LING; 2018).

Moreover, this type of content alters the way reality is perceived and confronted

by people, since the image of institutions is affected by this type of discourse

(CARNIELLI; EPSTEIN, 2019). Since there is confusion between beliefs, opinions, and arguments, discourses that seek to disinform stimulate an emotional

response to events in opposition to rationality.

Carnielli and Epstein (2019) define it as follows: a **belief** is a conviction

that someone accepts as true, while an **opinion** is a manifestation of that

belief. Neither are arguments. A **fact**, in turn, is something that can be proven

(for example, through repeated scientific studies, as is the case with global warming).

Facts guarantee the truth of premises, which form the basis of the **argument**.

Thus, invoking the maxim that "this is my opinion" is a disloyal conduct

completely removed from facts and argumentation.

**Figure 1** shows that, while arguments are based on facts that can

be proven true, opinions derive from beliefs.


**Figure 1 – Arguments and opinions**


Source: The author (2021) based on Carnielli and Epstein (2019).


Pieces of disinformation tend to be effective because they reproduce ideas and values

that were already being shared in specific niches (DOURADO, 2020).

Furthermore, on the platforms, algorithms are adapted to favor interpersonal communication, amplifying interactivity among users and the reinforcement of beliefs already established

in smaller groups. "Algorithms have therefore become pillars of content curation and audience fragmentation in social media to the point of shaping how

users are led to interact with each other and consume information" (DOURADO, 2020, p.

63).

Various cognitive biases are used to perpetuate disinformation,

such as confirmation bias — the acceptance of information with which one

already agrees (CARNIELLI; EPSTEIN, 2019; COSENZA, 2016). In an attempt to uncover

how beliefs are entrenched and how they spread in society, researchers

O'Connor and Weatherall (2019) seek explanations by examining social networks — not necessarily

digital ones. One of the network models worked by the authors concerns the

predisposition of a group to trust someone who shares the same perceptions as them

in some respect. For example, if a person holds conservative values (in favor of the traditional family and against abortion, by way of illustration) and sees that the president

shares the same premise, the tendency is to trust him with other decisions.

Other cognitive biases explored by Carnielli and Epstein (2019) are the bandwagon effect,

in which people believe something because many others believe it; the repetition cascade,

in which content becomes plausible due to repetitions in public

discourse; and the third-person effect, the belief that messages have more effect on others

than on oneself. One of the most common examples, explain Capella, Ophir and Sutton (2018),

involves cigarettes: a smoker knows that smoking is bad, but tends to think that it may

be harmful to most people who smoke, but not to him. Thus, the authors

understand that, to correct disinformation effectively, one must first measure

people's knowledge about a topic, stimulating different approaches to understand,

also, how much someone sees themselves as part of the problem.

One limitation, however, is that we learn from others. According to O'Connor

and Weatherall (2019, p. 8), most people acquire false beliefs in the same

places where they acquire reliable beliefs, and for this reason there is the risk of ending up learning both.

This is what makes _homo sapiens_ the winner of human evolution. "The ability to

share information and influence one another's beliefs is part of what makes

humans special." [22] Not infrequently, as Kakutani (2018) shows, populist politicians draw

on a "us versus them" discourse, attacking what is external to a small group and

reinforcing feelings of nationalism, tribalism, and hatred of foreigners.

There is also complexity regarding the reasons why people spread

disinformation — and do not necessarily believe it. The persistence of some stories

circulating, say O'Connor and Weatherall (2019), is related to the fact that some

people believe it to be something funny or unbelievable, and therefore share it

out of irony or sarcasm. Additionally, there are those who share

content because the meme or other digital piece is capable of reflecting their feelings

about a topic.

Specifically about Covid-19, Posetti and Bontcheva (2020) understand that there

are some strategies to correct and curb disinformation. For the authors, responses

to this public health problem can be structured around four fronts: monitoring

and investigation (fact-checking and _debunking_ — publicly rebutting rumors —

for example); State policies and legislation; curation, technology, and economic response; and

an ethical, educational, and credibility-related perspective.

For Carnielli and Epstein (2019), the evaluation of premises through critical

thinking is a way of combating disinformation. In this sense, the authors encourage

people to identify who benefits from the news or post, what its central

point and conclusions are, detection of fallacies in reasoning, differentiation between facts and

the interpretation of facts, quality of evidence, source credibility, comparison

with other sources, whether the story is too interesting or impressive, whether someone signs

the piece, among other measures.

Regarding Covid-19, two recent studies presented by Pennycook _et al._

(2020) involving more than 1,700 adults in the United States show that

people share disinformation in part because they did not sufficiently reflect

on the accuracy of the content they read and passed on. Thus, the authors showed that,

to stimulate critical thinking, one effective measure can be the practice of _nudge_,

that is, offering small stimuli for people to reflect before sharing

content. By analyzing _WhatsApp_ and _Facebook_ as environments where

22 Original: "The ability to share information and influence one another's beliefs is part of what makes
humans special."


the spread of disinformation occurs, Rossini _et al._ (2020) observed that corrections tend

to be made in the messaging app more frequently. Given that _WhatsApp_

is typically used among close contacts, it is regarded as a safer environment for making corrections.

One of the best-known practices within journalism as a form of combating

disinformation is _fact-checking_, that is, fact verification. This activity consists in the

verification of claims made by public figures and occurs mainly through

the comparison of data presented discursively with evidence found in public

databases and official sources. The origin of this practice is attributed to journalist

Brooks Jackson of CNN, in 1991 in the United States, in connection with electoral coverage

(AMAZEEN, 2015; GRAVES, 2016). It can be considered a journalistic genre that

moves between the political and academic spheres, since it involves public figures and is a practice

with a specific method of verification. As we shall see below, transparency,

associated with methodological objectivity, is one of the normative principles of this type of

journalism (SEIBT, 2019), similar to what occurs in Data-Driven Journalism

(GEHRKE, 2018b).

By analyzing changes in journalism, Seibt (2019) identified that _fact-che-_
