Transparency is at the center of the 2014 version of the Code of Ethics of the Society


of Professional Journalists of the United States. One section of the code is dedicated exclusively to this matter. According to the text, ethical journalism presupposes taking responsibility


for one's acts and explaining to the public the decisions made. Thus, from a normative standpoint, the code indicates that professionals should: explain ethical choices to the audience,


encouraging civil debate; respond quickly to questions about accuracy and clarity; identify and correct errors immediately, making the corrections made clear; and expose


any conduct that falls short of ethical standards in journalism (SOCIETY OF PROFESSIONAL JOURNALISTS, 2014). In the Code of Ethics of Brazilian Journalists [79],


79 Available at: https://fenaj.org.br/codigo-de-etica-dos-jornalistas-brasileiros/


made available by the National Federation of Journalists (Fenaj) and updated on August 4,


2007, there is no mention of transparency.

Some editorial guidelines and conduct manuals in journalism are outdated and do not


incorporate the Web environment as a publication space. In principle, this space, being


open, easily allows for the structuring of information in layers — through hypertext, as we pointed out earlier — and the rapid correction of errors. After analyzing


the error policies employed by five digital newspapers, Vieira (2014) suggested the need for an ethics code specific to this type of media in Brazil, as well as the


updating of editorial guidelines, since the available texts did not account for the


specificities of journalism practiced and published on the Web. In general, the results of her investigation indicated a lack of transparency, non-visible rectification, deficient revision,


haste, perpetuation of errors, original articles without any mention of the error, and a lack of interaction with the reader. She also found that when error correction occurs in a transparent manner, values such as trust and credibility are reinforced between the reader and the journalistic outlet (VIEIRA, 2014).


From the perspective of journalists, there is no doubt that transparency emerges


as a fundamental value (CHRISTOFOLETTI, 2021; GEHRKE, 2020). A study by Toff _et al_. (2021) that gathers the expectations of journalists and readers about how journalism should


be conducted concludes that transparency is a value widely invoked by professionals. However, this keyword is not cited by the audience — the public prioritizes familiarity with the brand and the reputation of the media outlet, also valuing


the way in which a news story is presented. In this study, focus groups were conducted


with people in different countries — including Brazil —, but it should be noted


that the concept of "transparency" may simply not be part of readers' vocabulary;


they invoke in their discourse values such as impartiality and balance.

Even though it may seem paradoxical, admitting error is one of the points most valued


by the public and taken into account when reflecting on the media outlets whose


news they will consume. Karlsson and Clerwall (2018) found that, although readers


criticize journalistic mistakes — which in principle should not occur, being prevented


by the fact-checking process —, they value the honesty of journalists and outlets when


they admit they were wrong. Thus, it is possible to think that a trust relationship


is built between audiences and media outlets; like any interpersonal relationship,


trust presents itself as a fundamental element. "Even if transparency is not always a sufficient condition for more


ethical behavior, its absence is a prerequisite for deception [...]" [80] (PLAISANCE, 2007, p. 193). If


transparency is not a complete antidote to failures, its absence becomes a risk.


Since journalism's purpose is to inform society, it is to this public


that it must show itself to be honest, bound to ethical principles, and accountable. "If a fact


was reported in a partial or inaccurate manner, if an article or broadcast disclosed some


information containing material errors, the facts must be clarified and the errors corrected" (CORNU, 1998, p. 68-69). Ethical conduct, even if it seems centered on the practice of reporting, must extend to editorial decision-making that guides the entire


conduct of a journalistic company. This involves, for example, making clear who finances the newspaper, since financial matters tend to raise questions about


possible conflicts of interest.


**4.3.2 Discursive resource of legitimation**


At the organizational level, transparency can serve as a strategy of legitimation


for maintaining perceived credibility [81] (LISBOA, 2012) among readers. In other


words, a media outlet opens up its methods, processes, and editorial matters


not only because it is ethically advisable, as discussed in the previous section, but also in order to _appear_ transparent. Editorial transparency is associated with the reliability


of the information produced and disseminated by media outlets (GUERRA, 2014)


and depends on the trust relationship between the public and journalists.


In an era in which information proliferates online,
in which everyone claims the status of information producer,
transparency about who is credentialed to
provide this service and the way in which they do so can be a decisive
attitude for distinguishing those worthy of credibility from the rest
(GUERRA, 2014, p. 207-208).


80 In the original: "Even if transparency is not always a sufficient condition for more ethical behavior, its absence is a prerequisite for deception [...]."
81 In her master's research, Lisboa (2012) identified five core values that readers typically associate with trustworthy journalism: independence, impartiality, honesty, objectivity, and coherence.


From a discursive standpoint, transparency will never be complete.


The journalist chooses what they wish to communicate and decides how much of their methods will be known by the audience. Moreover, there is no literality in discourse, and meanings will always be produced by a subject who interprets. Journalism, as a


particular discursive genre, produces narratives under very specific conditions. Based on its methods of fact-gathering, the cross-referencing of sources, the plurality of versions, and


the defense of objectivity, it adopts fundamental strategies to construct its own narration before the public (BENETTI, 2008). In any case, when it appears in the discourse of journalists, transparency also affects their practices (ALLEN, 2008).


When it chooses not to open to the public its decisions, its reporting methods, and


any limits that may arise in its productions, journalism seeks to maintain its role of authority and ensure the legitimacy needed to continue disseminating only


what suits it (BENETTI, 2008). The constructed narrative thus aims to promote an


effect of truth and ensure the credibility of the one who speaks (in this case, journalism itself). In this way, transparency can also be thought of as a discursive


strategy of legitimation associated with an _effect_ — even if the public does not reproduce the methods detailed in the news, the mere fact of having this option available confers


an effect of transparency (LISBOA, FRANZONI, 2018). And, even though it is difficult to measure real


gains in credibility, the demonstration of transparency can serve to allow journalism to simply maintain the _status quo_ of qualified mediator of reality on


topics of public interest, avoiding drastic changes in the way the public perceives it


(KARLSSON, 2022).


When discussing journalism as a justified true belief, Lisboa and


Benetti (2015) understand that the transparency effect falls within the scope of justification. Thus, the journalistic account presents clues and proofs that it speaks the truth, sustaining the credibility of the discourse. In this case, as Weinberger (2009, n.p.) says, the evidence can be even more easily accessed by the public, becoming something tangible: "[...] transparency mirrors the nature of the Web: Content is linked, public, discussed and always subject to dispute and revision." [82]

For Guerra (2014), the credibility of the journalistic organization comes from a series of pieces of information that attest to its integrity (the absence of conflicts of interest, for


82 In the original: "[…] transparency mirrors the nature of the Web: Content is linked, public, discussed and always subject to dispute and revision."


example) and data that guarantee the fulfillment of editorial principles in the journalistic product. Thus, transparency as a discursive resource of legitimation includes the demonstration that the news observed rigorous reporting methods, fulfilling the premises of truth, relevance, and plurality that accompany journalism. Moreover,


the promotion of transparency causes journalistic organizations to credential themselves


as relevant mediators in the democratic environment. As Guerra (2014, p. 208) says:


"The less transparency, the greater the risk of losing credibility."


The meaning of _accountability_ is linked to transparency as a resource of legitimation of its practices insofar as this concept refers to the rendering of accounts and responsibility, values frequently associated with the practices of public bodies. For Fengler (2019), _accountability_ is a broader concept than transparency, as it involves making public information about editorial processes and the actors involved. It is in this


perspective, mainly from the 1970s onward, that journalistic companies operate


with the aforementioned role of _ombudsman_ — the person who plays the role of reader's advocate.

In digital journalism, there is also discussion of _algorithm accountability_ [83] (DIAKOPOULOS, 2019)


to discuss not only the data analysis processes themselves, but the way


in which data are produced and structured.


In journalism studies, Porlezza and Splendore (2016, p. 199) cite codes of


ethics, collaborative news production, and tools that allow reader participation


as instruments of _accountability_. Digital platforms, such as social networks, are also seen by journalists as a way of getting closer to the audience (FENGLER, 2019). For _accountability_ to make sense and have applicable instruments, institutions need to have autonomy and be fully functioning, starting with democracy and the resulting culture of public participation.


To cite some examples, journalistic companies create products in which they seek


to render accounts to society, especially when the reader finances journalism. In some European countries, one adopted strategy is to publish annual editorial reports.


This is a kind of general balance sheet about what worked and what did not


83 Diakopoulos (2019, p. 11) refers to "_algorithm accountability reporting_" as the development of methods to audit and explain the formation of systems and databases in the face of processes of error, discrimination, and censorship that may be inherent to the reporting process.
for a given newsroom over the course of a year, including writing errors, ethical decisions, and, in positive cases, awards received by the journalism team. "Editorial


84 Diakopoulos (2019, p. 11) refers to "_algorithm accountability reporting_" as the development of methods to audit and explain the formation of systems and databases in the face of processes of error, discrimination, and censorship that may be inherent to the reporting process.


reports serve to legitimate journalism under new circumstances where the demand for such justification is required" [84] (EIDE, 2014, p. 683). By making this kind of self-assessment public, journalism asks for a vote of confidence and defends itself as credible.


An initiative responsible for investigations using open sources, _Bellingcat_ [85] published


a report with an annual balance [86] of its journalistic practices and revenues for


the year 2020. Since the public is an important source of funding for this


outlet, there is a commitment to provide some form of accountability. Once outlets


make their operating premises clear, readers are able to assess whether there is


any type of conflict of interest or dubious source of funding. The more


information they have, the more readers will be able to analyze, on their own, what the outlet has done with the resources received. Even if _accountability_ practices are


associated with a _transparency effect_, such an effect can generate implications for the way


in which audiences place trust in a journalistic outlet.


Research such as that of Johnson and St. John (2021) and Moran (2020) has demonstrated


that not only newspapers are the protagonists of perceived credibility; the journalists responsible for the news are also important in this process. It matters to know, for


example, who is behind the stories, their expertise, and even possible biases.


In research seeking to associate degrees of transparency with credibility perceived


by readers in relation to the outlet, Johnson and St. John (2021) examine the perceptions


of 885 readers in the United States. The audience was divided into eight different groups


to analyze what type of information is useful for broadening the perception of credibility in relation to the outlet. In one group, for example, the audience analyzed only the news story;


in another, the story, a photo, and extensive information about the reporter; in yet another, an explanatory box about the topic of the report was added, and so on.


The authors found that, in addition to the story itself and an explanatory box about why the story became news, featuring a photo of the journalist who wrote the story,


along with some details about them (education, _hobbies_, and professional experience), can


amplify the perception of credibility regarding the journalist, the story, and the organization for which the journalist works. Even though individually each of these aspects is capable


84 In the original: "Editorial reports serve to legitimate journalism under new circumstances where the demand for such justification is required".
85 Based in the Netherlands, _Bellingcat_ describes itself as an independent collective formed by researchers, investigators, and citizen journalists who use open sources and social networks to investigate various topics.
[86 Available at: https://www.bellingcat.com/app/uploads/2021/05/Bellingcat-Annual-Report-2020.pdf](https://www.bellingcat.com/app/uploads/2021/05/Bellingcat-Annual-Report-2020.pdf)


of adding value to the whole, it is in their combination that the best result is achieved.


This result offers clues about how transparency can be beneficial: "The key seems to be


finding a balance between disclosing information about the journalist and combining it


with information about why a story entered journalistic coverage" (JOHNSON;


ST JOHN, 2021, P. 967).


Moran (2020), in turn, examined the use of the _Slack_ platform [87] — which operates


as a kind of forum and chat — as a space for providing behind-the-scenes information about the news production process (_newsmaking_). The researcher analyzed


_Rantt Media_, a self-described independent outlet that seeks to combat disinformation


and extremism, located in Washington D.C., United States. Through interviews with journalists and readers and by following publications on Slack over five months,


she found that readers use the platform for two main reasons: a) as an aggregator of trustworthy news; b) as a space to get closer to the newsroom and understand who the journalists that work there are and what their perspectives are.


Among the readers interviewed, however, a high demand for


transparency was not detected. One reason for this may be the prior existence of a close and trusting relationship between readers and _Rantt Media_. In any case, the author identifies the


opening of a space on Slack as a significant act of transparency — or transparency effect, as I would define this case —, since such an attitude demonstrates to readers


and potential readers that the content is open to public scrutiny.


In other words, this means that transparency as a discursive resource of


legitimation is an invitation for the reader to get to know the practical and editorial processes insofar as it suggests the publication of qualified, satisfactory, and credible content. Associated with scientific knowledge through objectivity in method, Data-Driven


Journalism treats transparency as an important and defining feature of its structure.


87 _Slack_ is a business communications platform developed by _Slack Technologies_. It offers the creation of channels (similar to chat rooms) organized by topics of interest, private groups, and direct messages.


**4.3.3 Transparency in Data-Driven Journalism**


In order to execute the science-inspired method and, consequently, generate a type


of specialized knowledge (TRÄSEL; GEHRKE, 2021), Data-Driven Journalism


must submit its work to public scrutiny, offering audiences information


about its practices (operator of an ethical practice) and editorial principles (discursive resource of legitimation). Transparency thus functions as a reflection of truth and objectivity.


By operating primarily on the Web, DDJ inherits a tradition developed since


the time of its emergence in the 2000s: transparency through the use of hyperlinks.


At the time, blog authors used this openness resource to ensure the credibility of their publications (MEIER, 2009), just as they viewed their own work as


a construction, subject to frequent updates and therefore corresponding to a


provisional truth (PHILLIPS, 2010). Offering audiences the possibility of retracing the


steps adopted means acknowledging that the public has the right to access the same


information as journalists. "While reporters have to rely on second and third-hand knowledge, the public itself is even


more poorly equipped to assess the truthfulness of news" [88] (KOVACH; ROSENSTIEL, 2011, p. 33).

Creator of Precision Journalism, the main precursor of DDJ, Meyer (2002) understands that journalists and scientists share similar premises in carrying out their


work. Both categories of professionals, according to the author, adopt in their work: skepticism, by questioning the information received; openness, by explaining the investigation procedures through documents; an instinct for operationalization, since


they seek to test a hypothesis in the real world; a sense of obtaining truth, taking


into account that truth is provisional; and parsimony, since they remain attentive


to selecting theories that truly have the potential to explain a phenomenon.

By working with spreadsheets, mathematical models, statistical analysis, script formulations, and more — all under the hacker ethic of sharing —, it is expected


that journalists exchange information among themselves and are able to resolve doubts and advance knowledge as a community. The daily routine of newsrooms, however, seems


88 In the original: "While reporters have to rely on second and third-hand knowledge, the public itself is even more poorly equipped to assess the truthfulness of news".


to generate a negative impact on the potential of Data-Driven Journalism with regard


to the transparency policies adopted in news articles.

One of the few studies that examines transparency in DDJ is that of Rodrigo Zamith


(2019). By analyzing about 150 articles from _The New York Times_ and _The Washington_


_Post_ in the first half of 2017 using the concepts of transparency, interactivity,


diversity, and provenance of information, Zamith (2019) concludes that the DDJ practiced in


everyday work is not open and shows itself to be highly dependent on official sources. The investigation


also showed that it is rare for data journalists themselves to carry out the


collection of information. The author therefore understands that DDJ still needs to be improved,


characterizing it as a practice that is evolving but not revolutionary.

The results of Zamith's research converge with the critique made by Träsel


(2014): by omitting the original statistical models and databases, data journalists and their respective outlets fail to encourage the public auditing character that


transparency seeks to enable. The author illustrates that, in the case of science —


probably the main reference for DDJ —, articles are peer-reviewed and submitted to methodological evaluation by others, validating or not the findings made. "In the case of DDJ, access to the original databases would allow the audience and other


reporters to conduct their own analyses and judge the validity of the conclusions derived from a given journalistic piece" (TRÄSEL, 2014, p. 128).


It is clear that, in the face of political and public health issues, such as the Covid-19 pandemic itself, pressure increases for the opening of public data and methods used in analyses. In a study on DDJ coverage of the pandemic, Brenol and Träsel


(2020) analyzed reports using DDJ techniques and detected, among the results, the


lack of availability of consolidated information about Covid-19, pointing to the still


present difficulty of obtaining access to public information in Brazil. In this way,


according to the authors, journalists not only exposed these limitations but also adopted


a critical tone regarding access to data. There is a fear that this scenario is not


exclusive to the Covid-19 pandemic, but indicates greater opacity in the Brazilian state,


despite advances in digital public transparency in recent decades.

It is observed that, in recent years, journalistic initiatives anchored in the


principle of transparency have emerged. Journalists themselves, as we will see below, present


this characteristic markedly in their professional _ethos_. In the wake of collaboration and


the hacker ethic diagnosed by Träsel (2014) in Brazil, transparency adds itself as


a value invoked by the data journalism community.

In Brazil, the _Vortex Media_ initiative, launched in October 2019 by Diego Escosteguy, emerged to offer what it claimed to be a "journalism in motion" under the


discourse of "radical transparency of methods and intentions" (GEHRKE; BENETTI, 2020), but


never revealed its forms of financing and partially ceased operations two


months after its launch. As a result, the team of data journalists, one of the main differentiating features of the new enterprise, had only a short trajectory.

On January 6, 2020, after the closure of the _Vortex_ data team, the former editor of that small group, journalist Sérgio Spagnuolo, created together


with Alexandre Orrico the _Núcleo Jornalismo_. The outlet's mission, according to its own


page [89], is to place transparency at the center of everything in order to foster the use of "[...] mainly public data, through investigations, analyses, and applications that will inform


and shed light on public policies, institutions, and political and social events"


(NÚCLEO JORNALISMO, 2021, n.p.).


In this context, _Núcleo_ seeks to practice, internally, the same transparency it demands


from public authorities. "[...] the transparency we want from institutions is also what we must apply internally. This means corporate transparency (who funds us and who we are) and process transparency (how we do our journalism)" (NÚCLEO JORNALISMO,


2021, n.p.). To achieve this, the journalistic company declares a commitment to the systematic referencing of sources, including descriptions and links; transparency about public policies; and explanation of the importance of a topic.


In the United States, the digital native _The Markup_ [90], which describes itself as a nonprofit institution, created its own methodology to cover the area of technology and how


the decisions of major players (_Facebook_ and _Google_, for example) affect life in society. The _Markup Method_, according to editor Julia Angwin (2021), consists of the detailed publication of the methodology used in the reports. There are three main steps toward the scientific method: the elaboration of original data, peer review by specialists, and the communication of the data.

Despite these initiatives, conventional journalistic newsrooms are still not completely enveloped in the aura of transparency, although there is a movement of


89 Available at: https://nucleo.jor.br/sobre
90 Available at: https://themarkup.org/about


gradual change in this direction. Due to commercial interests, for example, some


media outlets choose not to open up their procedures. This premise aligns with what research conducted with Brazilian data journalists in 2019


(GEHRKE, 2020) shows. The survey suggests that transparency is more connected to the _ethos_ of the journalist than to the rules adopted in newsrooms. Almost three-quarters


(72.2%) of respondents agreed with the statement that "Some media outlets avoid being transparent (offering the source code, for example) due to competitive concerns."

After interviewing 17 journalists from 15 media outlets in Germany, Koliska


and Chadha (2018) found that, for the majority of those interviewed, transparency


is related to the disclosure of behind-the-scenes information to the public, as well as being linked to values such as honesty (including about possible biases of journalists in their news coverage). Even though journalists consider that greater


transparency can boost credibility in journalism, they state that the topic is rarely discussed in newsrooms. A topic that is little debated is, consequently, little implemented.

Among data journalists, studies by Gehrke (2020), with Brazilian professionals,


and Beiler, Irmer, and Breda (2020), with German professionals, show that practitioners understand that opening up the method is a good alternative for generating trust and quality.


While the Brazilians see transparency in Data-Driven Journalism as helping to establish a relationship of respect with the audience and as a way of combating


disinformation through its methodological clarity, the Germans understand that DDJ


produces more transparent practices than conventional journalism.

Research conducted with 36 data journalists [91] in Brazil (GEHRKE, 2020) shows that, among professionals, there is a high degree of confidence in the capacity of DDJ to be an


open practice capable of demonstrating methods and procedures. More than three-quarters


(80.6%) of participants agreed with the statement that "With scientific roots,


data journalism is one of the most efficient ways of presenting transparent content, since its methods allow for replication," while 19.4%


91 The research involved the administration of a questionnaire to Brazilian data journalists. The invitation to participate was sent to approximately 200 professionals, but only 36 responded. Of these, half (50%) report working in newsrooms, while a quarter (25%) say they are entrepreneurs and 8.3% are freelancers. The remaining 16.7% correspond to trainees, public servants, project coordinators, and similar activities. Regarding the period of work, 38.9% have been journalists for more than ten years, 33.3% for six to ten years, and 27.8% have up to five years in the profession (GEHRKE, 2020).


declared themselves neutral in relation to this statement. However, no one disagreed. Furthermore,


many participants (91.7%) indicated that transparency should be demonstrated


on a daily basis, in all texts, while 8.3% believe this should only happen in special reports.


For the Brazilian professionals consulted in the survey, transparency is


considered an important value in journalism because [92]: it contributes to increasing credibility (91.7%); it shows the evidence of a fact (86.1%); it helps in combating disinformation (80.6%); it increases the quality of content (80.6%); it helps to create a


relationship of respect with the reader (75%); it shows the reader which source was consulted (52.8%); and it involves


opening up the source code used (47.2%).

In an interview with Gehrke and Mielniczuk (2017, p. 10), Meyer identifies clarity in


the use of journalistic sources as one of the main virtues for methodological replicability: "The best journalism describes its methods with such clarity and precision that the


reader is convinced that they could follow the same procedures and reach the same conclusions." Phillips (2010) goes beyond the basics: for the author, the ideal would be for media outlets to publish the metadata — the data about the data — to organize information, show original references, and structure them in a way that readers


can understand them in their entirety. When journalism fulfills the requirements of transparency,


especially regarding the opening of methods and sources, it draws closer to science.


At this point, the key word is **replicability**, whose centrality will be demonstrated in the


final proposition of this thesis.


92 In this question, a list was presented to participants, who could check more than one item about why they consider transparency important.


**5 ELEMENTS OF TRANSPARENCY: METHODOLOGICAL PATH AND RESULTS**


Monitored by the press worldwide, the Covid-19 pandemic demanded an explanatory and specialized coverage from journalists. Data-Driven Journalism


teams were called upon, at first, to count and contextualize the


number of confirmed cases and deaths from Covid-19. The lack of tests and reliable data


proved to be a problem in different countries, challenging journalists to work with


the available information and to acknowledge the limitations of official statistics. Mainly


in the second year of the pandemic, the numbers on vaccination also began to be


consulted, adding challenges inherent to the quality of public data [93] and the need


for scientific knowledge to understand and explain the efficacy of vaccines.

In Brazil, the federal government became the biggest obstacle to qualified journalistic coverage, especially at the beginning of the pandemic. At the time, the Ministry of Health


presented a limited dashboard [94], using only aggregated data and


disregarding the progression of the disease in municipalities. It also ignored the need to record a


historical series of cases and deaths. This type of longitudinal record is essential for


understanding the progression of Covid-19 in different parts of Brazil and adopting strategies for combating the spread of the virus.


In early June 2020, President Jair Bolsonaro was responsible for promoting a kind of "data blackout," ordering the Ministry of Health to delay


the timing of information releases — which harmed the press and the compilation of


numbers for news programs — and to modify the Covid-19 dashboard, limiting


public access to daily data without reporting the accumulated total of cases and deaths.

In response, journalistic outlets created a task force [95] to update cases and deaths daily, based on numbers from municipalities and states. The consortium is


formed by _O Estado de S. Paulo_, _Extra_, _Folha de S.Paulo_, _O Globo_, _G1_, and _UOL_ and, although


it was created to bring transparency to pandemic data, its organization is


only internal, since the data are not open to the public. At the beginning of November


93 In Brazil, the health system is tripartite and, therefore, updates on the number of cases and deaths and on vaccination require mobilization of the Executive Branch at its different levels. Municipal health departments report to state health departments, which in turn communicate information to the Ministry of Health.
94 Available at: https://covid.saude.gov.br/
95 Available at: https://www1.folha.uol.com.br/equilibrioesaude/2020/06/veiculos-de-comunicacaoformam-parceria-para-dar-transparencia-a-dados-de-covid-19.shtml


of 2020, a new technical problem was attributed to the Ministry of Health's system, causing notifications from Brazilian states about new cases and deaths


to be delayed, and once again undermining the count related to the progression of the disease. The problems diminished but did not cease in the second year of the pandemic: on various occasions, information about the pandemic went offline on the ministry's website.


The complex scenario that presents itself in Brazil regarding the availability and


access to public Covid-19 data, and how much this interferes with journalistic coverage,


motivated the delimitation of the thesis corpus. For this reason, the proposal is to study journalistic texts about Covid-19 that employed Data-Driven Journalism


techniques and were published in reference newspapers and digital natives. The


corpus includes only informational journalism materials — that is, news articles and reports —


as well as journalistic projects made with the use of data and elaborated exclusively for the


coverage of Covid-19.


This qualitative investigation presents a series of methodological procedures


for gathering and analyzing the elements of transparency employed by Brazilian newspapers during coverage of Covid-19 in 2020 and 2021. Since


transparency in Data-Driven Journalism is the theoretical object of investigation, the analysis begins with a bibliographic review to establish the state of the art in this field and identify its gaps. For this reason, this is an exploratory study, whose main purpose is to


provide an overview of a topic that is still little studied. This is a


research that systematizes knowledge and can serve as the basis for new studies, with


new hypotheses. Gil (2019b, p. 26) says of exploratory research: "The final product


of this process becomes a more clearly defined problem, amenable to investigation through more systematic procedures."


Concurrent with the literature review present before and during the process of


writing the thesis, I conducted systematic observation of informational journalistic products for


the composition of an intentional sample (GIL, 2019b). In this thesis, we use


as our main methodology the multiple case study (STAKE, 2006) insofar as


transparency presents itself as the central object of study. The selection of cases is thus


formed by informational journalistic products that present elements of transparency as a common characteristic.

Although the discussion of transparency has been ongoing for about two decades in


journalism studies, it is still quite recent in Data-Driven Journalism. For this reason, this research draws inspiration from methodological efforts previously undertaken in the field of digital journalism, such as the thesis of Barbosa (2007). Of an innovative character, the author


chose to select representative cases — the case study as illustration (MACHADO; PALACIOS, 2007) — to ground the conceptual discussions of the Digital Database Journalism Paradigm (JDBD).


In this investigation, the multiple case study allows for the description of


transparency characteristics that are inherent to news articles and reports developed based on


Data-Driven Journalism techniques. To exemplify each element described, we


use the case study as illustration — in the next section, we will detail these methodological choices.

After the systematization and description of the elements of transparency, I formulate the following proposals: the **Transparency Protocol in Data-Driven Journalism**


for analyzing journalistic products already published, whose structure is inspired by the Protocol


for Analysis of Journalistic Coverage (SILVA; MAIA, 2011), and the **Method for Data-Driven**


**Journalism with a focus on transparency and reproducibility**. Both will be


described in the sixth chapter of the thesis.


**5.1 Multiple case study and as illustration**


This thesis, with its qualitative approach, presents two main methodological inspirations: the multiple case study (STAKE, 2006) for the analysis of phenomena, and the


hybrid modality of the case study as illustration (MACHADO; PALACIOS, 2007) adopted by researchers of the Online Journalism Research Group (GJOL) at the


Federal University of Bahia (UFBA). Both procedures are particularly useful for


the systematic observation of digital empirical objects still in development.


In the hybrid methodology of the case study as illustration, three main stages


occur, according to Machado and Palacios (2007, p. 343): a preliminary review of the bibliography and analysis of journalistic organizations; delimitation of the object with the formulation of hypotheses and the case study combined with research within journalistic organizations; and, finally,


the elaboration of categories of analysis for processing the collected material and conceptual definitions about the objects of study. "Our methodology allows the researcher,


on the one hand, to review the current bibliography on the object and, on the other, enables this


conceptual production to be tested in specific case studies." In this thesis, I take the


hybrid methodology as an inspiration because there is no observation of the productive processes within journalistic newsrooms as such. Here, I analyze only the product.


The multiple case study (STAKE, 2006), in turn, is used to examine


a phenomenon that can be explored from different angles or parts. This is


an effective approach for the analysis of phenomena. In this thesis, the phenomenon studied


is transparency in Data-Driven Journalism, whose intentional sample


(GIL, 2019b), which is non-probabilistic, involves the selection of cases that are similar to each other. In the


present research, this materializes through informational journalistic products, namely news articles, reports, and data-driven projects.

Although in this methodology each case is studied in a distinct manner, the aim is


to understand how individual cases can contribute to the understanding of the phenomenon


as a whole. In the multiple case study, the object intended for study is called


the quintain (_quintain_, in English), formed by singular cases that share some


characteristic in common. In this study, all the news articles or reports that make up the


_corpus_ have elements of transparency as a common thread. In general, four to ten cases are selected to compose an investigation guided by this method. This is because, in a single product, it becomes difficult to find all the characteristics one intends to study.


For Stake (2006), one of the advantages of adopting the multiple case study is the


fact that it can take into account the context or the system in a broad manner, characteristics that


are sometimes lost in the conventional case study. Both, of course, present specific limitations — both the case study and the multiple case study correspond


to a particular reality and do not present external validity, even though they may recur in other circumstances. This type of method is therefore adopted to understand


a specific phenomenon belonging to a particular frame. Thus, it is an alternative


for obtaining answers motivated by a central object that is not necessarily broad.

At this point, the hybrid methodology proposed in the case study as illustration


(MACHADO; PALACIOS, 2007) converges with the purpose of the multiple case study methodology. As there is no exhaustive description of the objects as occurs in the conventional case


study, the case study as illustration is a stage in the methodological process used to illustrate arguments and demonstrate the validity of or refute hypotheses. In this


way, this methodology presents a somewhat open format, which allows for


future formulations to encompass aspects hitherto disregarded by researchers.


As indicated earlier, the empirical corpus of the thesis is made up of informational materials published in 2020 and 2021 by Brazilian reference newspapers and digital natives, with the Covid-19 pandemic as their main topic. The analysis period of the thesis corpus begins in March 2020, when the first


reports on the topic were detected, and ends in August 2021, due to the thesis deadline, since


the coverage of the Covid-19 pandemic has no set end date. I analyze only


journalistic material already published, without taking into account the stage of news production.


To select the texts that make up this convenience sample, I conducted, throughout the pandemic, free browsing and systematic observation that allowed me to identify, at the product level, aspects related to transparency in journalism in light of


theory. And, for the purposes of methodological rigor, I observe guidelines for corpus composition


for qualitative research, in which materials present only one thematic focus and, therefore, relevance, homogeneity — the analysis is restricted to texts, for example — and


synchronicity, which refers to the presence of the time frame in history (BAUER; AARTS, 2015).

Regarding the _corpus_ selected to compose the multiple case analysis, the segmentation is quite clear: synchronicity is ensured by the selection of texts referring to the


same historical period — that is, the beginning and development of the Covid-19 pandemic. Moreover, the news articles and reports were published in Portuguese and within the Brazilian context.


The _corpus_ is made up of journalistic texts published by the following media outlets: _Agência Lupa_ [96], _Agência Pública_ [97], _El País_ _[98]_ _(Brasil)_, _Fiquem Sabendo_ [99], _Folha de_


96 _Agência Lupa_ is a Brazilian digital fact-checking agency founded in November 2015. It has 16 professionals and presents an educational arm to promote training in fact-checking and debunking. Available at: https://piaui.folha.uol.com.br/lupa/
97 _Agência Pública_ is a digital native founded in 2011, and self-described as the first non-profit investigative journalism agency in Brazil. Its site has a specific section for the publication of data-driven reports. Available at: https://apublica.org/
98 _El País Brasil_ is the digital version of the _El País_ website, a Spanish newspaper founded in 1976. It published occasional articles in Portuguese using DDJ techniques. The Brazilian newsroom ceased operations at the end of 2021. Available at: https://brasil.elpais.com/
99 _Fiquem Sabendo_ is an agency specialized in the Freedom of Information Act and therefore gathers requests through the Citizen Information Service (SIC) and publicly offers the data obtained from its own investigations. This initiative began operating unofficially in 2015, and was recognized as a business from 2018 onward. Available at: https://fiquemsabendo.com.br/


_S.Paulo_ [100], _GaúchaZH_ [101], _Jota_ [102], _Nexo_ [103], _Núcleo_ [104] _,_ _O Estado de S. Paulo_ _[105]_, and _Piauí_ [106]. Although


some of these outlets have professionals or teams that work exclusively


with the practice of Data-Driven Journalism, only _Núcleo_ presents an emphasis


on the production of materials derived from DDJ techniques. The _Fiquem Sabendo_ agency, while specialized in the Freedom of Information Act, acts much more as a source for other outlets, occasionally publishing reports on its own site. Some of the newspapers


chosen also participate in the _Projeto Credibilidade_ [107], which seeks to offer tools and techniques to broaden transparency in journalism.

The news articles and reports that served as the basis for the systematization of the elements of transparency in Data-Driven Journalism and that will be presented below


represent, obviously, only part of the possibilities of a study of this nature.


In other words, this research proposes to detect elements of transparency in light of theory — it does not mean that these are the only possible elements to be found


in digital journalism or Data-Driven Journalism.

The choice of Brazilian newspapers was made because DDJ is a growing practice in


the country — in 2021 alone, ten national projects were among the finalists of the largest


100 The newspaper _Folha de S.Paulo_ had its print version launched in 1921, and its website was created in 1995 under the name _Folha Online_. It publishes about 160 news items daily, and has a team specifically dedicated to data work: _Delta Folha_. Available at: https://www.folha.uol.com.br/
101 The _GZH_ (GaúchaZH) portal is a journalistic website that has existed since 2017 and is part of _Grupo RBS_, which also includes the newspaper formerly known as _Zero Hora_, founded in May 1964. In 2020, the newspaper created the _Fluência em Notícias_ project to show audiences how its news production works. Available at: https://gauchazh.clicrbs.com.br/
102 _Jota_ is a digital journalistic company founded in 2014 to cover the Brazilian Judiciary. It has a specific section for the publication of data-driven reports. Available at: https://www.jota.info/
103 _Nexo_ is a digital newspaper launched in November 2015 with the aim of providing context to news and broadening public access to data and statistics. Available at: https://www.nexojornal.com.br/
104 _Núcleo_ is a journalistic website launched on January 6, 2020, to lead investigations based on data, seeking to bring more transparency to government, political debate, and public policies. Transparency is the central point of its mission. Available at: https://nucleo.jor.br/
105 The newspaper _O Estado de S. Paulo_ has been published in print since January 4, 1875, during the Brazilian Empire (at the time its name was _A Província de S. Paulo_; the current name came in January 1890). Subsequently, it also began publishing news online. Its website has 1995 as its starting year. In its team, DDJ productions are associated with the Infographics section. Available at: https://www.estadao.com.br/
106 Launched in 2006 as a print magazine — still in circulation, it should be noted — _Piauí_ also has a specific section for DDJ publications, called Igualdades. Available at: https://piaui.folha.uol.com.br/
107 The _Projeto Credibilidade_, also known by its English name — Trust Project — has been developing, since 2016, transparency standards to help the public evaluate the quality and credibility of journalism. Among the Brazilian outlets mentioned in this thesis, the following newspapers participate in the project: _Agência Lupa_, _Folha de S.Paulo_, _GaúchaZH_, and _Nexo_. The _Projeto Credibilidade_ indicators for fostering the opening of processes and methods involve the clear identification of the following topics: 1) Best practices; 2) Journalist; 3) Type of content; 4) Citations and references; 5) Method; 6) Local reporting; 7) Diversity of voices; and 8) Actionable feedback. Available at: https://www.credibilidade.org/


data journalism award in the world, called the _Sigma Awards_ —, because DDJ developed in Latin America is still little studied (DE LIMA; MESQUITA, 2021), and because


I know the context of journalistic publication in Brazil during the Covid-19 pandemic with considerable authority. Involving publications from other countries, though it could bring original contributions and broaden the transparency elements detected, would demand a very specific situational analysis for each of those nations, given that journalistic production depends on the context in which it occurs.


Just as Barbosa (2007), who sought innovative and distinguishing examples for the composition of empirical material in light of theory, I selected journalistic cases


that allowed me to observe the articulation of transparency elements in the coverage


of Covid-19. Some journalistic outlets recur among the examples presented, due


to the higher frequency of use of certain elements of transparency. In an initial mapping, I identified about 150 informational materials observed mainly


among these 11 media outlets, reading the texts and selecting


the most relevant cases to compose the empirical corpus of this thesis. When possible, I


restricted my searches to the Data-Driven Journalism sections offered by the companies


(such as _Delta Folha_ at _Folha de S.Paulo_, the _Igualdades_ section at _Piauí_, and the _Dados_ area at _Agência Pública_).


Finally, we note that the advantage of presenting a set formed from a convenience sample is its specificity and its relationship to the theoretical object addressed.


Moreover, the inspiration from the multiple case study (STAKE, 2006) and the case study


as illustration (MACHADO; PALACIOS, 2007) allows the formation of categories


to enable new research to advance toward a quantitative analysis stage beyond this initial systematization, generating new types of knowledge.


**5.2 Transparency and its levels: organizational and operational**


Unlike its precursor known as Computer-Assisted Reporting (CAR), in which data were treated as just another source without necessarily taking center stage in the report, Data-Driven Journalism includes


transparency as a key aspect of its processes and results (CODDINGTON, 2014),


since, as we noted earlier, it is anchored in a philosophy of open source


and collaboration (TRÄSEL, 2014), derived from the community of developers and computer scientists.


For transparency to sustain itself as an **operator of an ethical practice** and a **discursive resource**


**of legitimation**, it must, strictly speaking, be an element that resonates both at the organizational level and at the operational level — in this case, it must also be reflected in


the product. This means that, ideally, it is not enough for journalists to adopt and demonstrate clarity in their methodological path. Journalistic companies must


offer clarity on broader topics, such as the error correction policy, the


diversity policy adopted in the company, and, no less importantly, their sources of financing. Knowledge about the origin of funding tends to be a good ally for


audiences in evaluating the type of coverage made (or ignored) by a newspaper.

As we will see below, it is unlikely that a single journalistic outlet will present, on its own, all possible and viable elements of transparency. Even though Covid-19 coverage is significant at the operational level, it is necessary to consider that the publication of


reports is part of a broader context of decisions and editorial processes present


in the newspaper as a whole. For this reason, it is impossible to ignore this prior stage, inherent to editorial decisions that reverberate throughout the entire organizational structure (the organizational level of transparency), and not only in the informational content of Data-Driven Journalism (the operational level of transparency).
of Data-Driven Journalism (the operational level of transparency).

Furthermore, the use of one or more elements of transparency does not guarantee,


necessarily, that the media outlet is attentive to the scientific premises linked


to Data-Driven Journalism, or that it follows all the methods suggested for this practice. The elements presented below aim at the systematization of knowledge and exemplification, and do not intend to point out that one media outlet is more or less transparent than another.


I present below the topics corresponding to the two levels verified with respect


to transparency in Data-Driven Journalism: **organizational** (regarding the outlet, also


involving aspects about the journalist who works in that organization) and **operational**


(regarding the method).

The listed examples follow the methodological inspiration of the multiple case


study (STAKE, 2006) and the case study as illustration (MACHADO; PALACIOS, 2007).

Due to the methodological choices of this thesis, the elements of transparency are listed


and analyzed individually and exclusively — that is, without the detailed explanations that normally occur in the case study. In some cases, each element


will have only the most significant example for its section — as recommended


by the multiple case study methodology, for which the understanding of a phenomenon


does not demand quantitative but qualitative exploration; there are some exceptions, however, in which more than one case is presented per element for contextual purposes.


It should be clarified, finally, that I am not evaluating whether a given element of


transparency is consistently used and updated within a journalistic company.

This research, like other qualitative studies, represents a broad snapshot of the moment at which the analysis was undertaken.


**5.2.1 Organizational level: the newspaper**


At the organizational level, transparency appears as a **discursive resource of legitimation** — that is, the degree to which a newspaper shows itself as transparent through its strategies


of engagement with audiences. In this section, I present eight elements related to the


posture of the professional and the media outlet: **byline, profile, and expertise**; **contact**;


**error correction**; **sources of funding**; **editorial guidelines and editorial principles**;


**diversity policy**; **privacy policy**; and **type of content**.


**5.2.1.1 Byline, profile, and expertise**


Indicating the name of the professional responsible for reporting a story is


typically a basic requirement present in editorial guidelines. As a transparency strategy, however, it must go further, also including information about the journalist's profile, such as work previously developed by them; alongside this description, aspects of their own training should appear, indicating their credentials.


If the skills and knowledge of the author of a report are publicly known,


the reader will be able to judge the level of specialization of the professional and


how prepared they are to cover a given topic.


For this reason, in this section, we present three characteristics that are intertwined: the


**byline**, which refers to the clarity of disclosure of the name of the person responsible for the


text; the **profile**, which concerns the possibility of accessing a page of the history of other reports published by that same person; and **expertise**, which shows the


competencies developed by the professional. Not all journalistic outlets will present all of these characteristics; the most common is to offer only the reporter's name,


without further detail.


In the following example, we observe the description of the reporter's profile in the publication


entitled _TrateCov indicava cloroquina e outros remédios sem eficácia a quase todos pacientes_ [TrateCov Indicated Chloroquine and Other Ineffective Drugs to Almost All Patients], published by _Jota_ (MARCELINO, 2021).


**Figure 8 – Reporter's prior knowledge is listed in a news item at** _**Jota**_


Source: _Jota_. Screenshot taken on May 18, 2021.


**Figure 8** prominently displays the author's name, just below the


title of the article, and further details about their area of training and expertise that functions as a footnote at the end of the text. There is, however, no hyperlink directing the public to learn about


other work developed by them.

In the case of Covid-19 and many other examples that require prior technical knowledge, this is a transparency strategy that speaks not only to the credibility of the outlet, but also to the reliability ensured by the reporter's credentials. In this particular report, the professional uncovered the source code used


by the Ministry of Health to recommend the use of medications with no proven efficacy to


supposedly prevent the disease. However, transparency stops there in this _Jota_ report: the source code examined in the article — corresponding to the element of reproducibility, as we will see below — is not even made public.

In another example, in the report entitled _Cidades mais desenvolvidas do país vacinam mais rápido contra a Covid_ [Most Developed Cities in the Country Vaccinate Against Covid More Quickly] (BARBON _et al_., 2021), published by _Folha de S.Paulo_,


the list of authors is disclosed at the end of the article (at the center of **Figure 9**).


**Figure 9 – Names of reporters serve as an indexer at** _**Folha**_


Source: _Folha de S.Paulo_. Screenshot taken on Aug. 13, 2021.


Highlighted at the center and right of **Figure 9**, it is possible to observe that the name of


each reporter functions, through a hyperlink, as a kind of index of


previous work. In addition, it is possible to find, alongside the name, the main professional information about the reporter (at the top of **Figure 9**, on the right).


Beyond information about the professionals responsible for the report, ideally the journalistic outlet should also include data about the editor who coordinates each section.


In general terms, readers are in a position to judge the reporter's work if the


main precepts of this section are fulfilled; less common, however, is public knowledge


about the work structure of a journalistic company, in which the news production chain is far more complex, always passing through one or more editors before


publication.


**5.2.1.2 Contact**


Contact lists and forms for newsrooms are not new in the Brazilian press


and represent a bridge between the journalistic outlet and the reader. This type of


openness becomes fundamental in times of health crisis, in which journalism reinforces


its role in providing relevant public information. The ways to contact the journalistic company to report an error or even comment on a story must therefore be


evident.


_Folha de S.Paulo_ offers a contact list through the _Fale com a Folha_ [Talk to the Folha] section,


available in the footer of the site [108]. The newspaper presents different communication channels with the reader, including contact via _WhatsApp_ and different email addresses that vary according to the section to be contacted. Moreover, _Folha_ is mentioned here as an example because it features the role of _ombudsman_, which appears as a contact


possibility in the footer of the site, and critically analyzes, in their column [109] (**Figure 10**), the journalistic content published.


**Figure 10 – Contact with the** _**ombudsman**_ **is one of the highlights at** _**Folha**_


Source: _Folha de S.Paulo_. Screenshot taken on Jul. 21, 2021.


108 Available at: https://www1.folha.uol.com.br/falecomafolha/
109 Available at: https://www1.folha.uol.com.br/ombudsman/


The role of _ombudsman_ has been held since May 10, 2021 by José Henrique


Mariante, with whom the reader can have direct contact via email or phone (highlighted


in the lower right corner in **Figure 10**); before him, Flávia Lima was responsible. _Folha_


was the first Brazilian newspaper to adopt this role, in 1989. In addition to serving as


a link between the reader and the newspaper, the figure of the _ombudsman_ is fundamental for demonstrating a publication's error correction, as we will see below.


**5.2.1.3 Error correction**


The error correction policy concerns the way in which the company deals with


reporting problems and lack of accuracy detected after the publication of content.


It is normally present on its own site and/or in spaces such as the editorial guidelines,


bringing together the step-by-step of how an error should be textually clarified and communicated to the reader. This is a basic element for an honest relationship with the public.


At _Nexo_, the error correction policy is explicit within the editorial standards section [110] and, when applied in articles that require some type of retraction,


an erratum is recorded at the end of the article. According to the material provided by the newsroom (at the top of **Figure 11**), the aim is to correct erroneous data immediately and transparently.


110 Available at: https://www.nexojornal.com.br/sobre/Padr%C3%B5es-editoriais-do-Nexo


**Figure 11 –** _**Nexo**_ **presents an erratum at the end of its articles**


Source: _Nexo Jornal_. Screenshot taken on Aug. 18, 2021, with edits by the author.


In the publication of March 29, 2021, entitled _Por que tanta gente ignora regras no pior momento da pandemia_ [Why So Many People Ignore Rules at the Worst Moment of the Pandemic] (ROCHA, 2021), shown from the center to the


lower portion of **Figure 11**, the newspaper presents a clear note explaining what was wrong


and what was modified in relation to the original article, also indicating the date and time of


the correction.

Similarly, _Folha de S.Paulo_ presents an open error correction policy,


whose decisions are detailed in the editorial project publicly available on its site [111]. Access to this material occurs through the page footer, where the reader first


finds the _Erramos_ [We Were Wrong] section [112], which lists the texts that have undergone some type of


change.

**Figure 12** shows an example of how this works in practice. On July 2,


2021, the newspaper published the report entitled _Registros indicam que milhares no Brasil tomaram vacina vencida contra Covid; veja se você é um deles_ [Records Indicate Thousands in Brazil Received Expired Covid Vaccine; Check If You Are One of Them] (GAMBA; RIGHETTI, 2021).


Based on data from the Ministry of Health, the text indicates that 26,000 doses of the AstraZeneca vaccine past their expiration date were administered in 1,532 municipalities.


111 Available at: https://temas.folha.uol.com.br/folha-projeto-editorial/manual-de-redacao-conduta/erramos.shtml
112 Available at: https://www1.folha.uol.com.br/erramos/


**Figure 12 –** _**Erramos**_ **is highlighted by** _**Folha**_ **alongside the date and at the end of the text**


Source: _Folha de S.Paulo_. Screenshot taken on Jul. 18, 2021.


On July 6, 2021, the newspaper added an erratum, whose notice is thereafter


displayed alongside the original publication date of the report (highlighted on the left in **Figure 12**) and at the end of the text (highlighted at the bottom of **Figure 12**). The correction


states the following: "An earlier version of this report failed to alert that the data


about 26,000 doses administered past their expiration date could be due to errors in the Ministry of Health's system. The text has been updated" (GAMBA; RIGHETTI, 2021, n.p.).

The brief correction by _Folha_ generated reader dissatisfaction, with criticism directed at the


original publication and the problems in the verification of the database that generated the


original report. _Ombudsman_ José Henrique Mariante discussed the case in his column of July 10,


2021, entitled "Jornalismo vencido" [Expired Journalism] [113].


Finally, at the end of informational texts, _Folha de S.Paulo_ presents icons


for sharing content on digital social networks, a space for comments,


and a button for reporting errors (in **Figure 13**, indicated by an arrow).


113 Available at: https://www1.folha.uol.com.br/colunas/jose-henrique-mariante-ombudsman/2021/07/jornalismo-vencido.shtml


**Figure 13 – Error-reporting button appears at** _**Folha**_


Source: _Folha de S.Paulo_. Screenshot taken on Oct. 18, 2021.


By clicking the _Erramos?_ [Did We Get It Wrong?] button, the reader finds a form (on the right in **Figure**


**13**) where they have the opportunity to describe the error found or be redirected for direct contact with the newspaper's _ombudsman_.


**5.2.1.4 Sources of funding**


Disclosing the origin of the money that sustains a journalistic enterprise is one


of the basic aspects of transparency, since the sustainability of a newsroom or


consortium of professionals is the first step toward carrying out any activity that


requires remuneration. Tracing the origin of funding allows, in short, for readers to evaluate the existence of conflicts of interest, for example. It must therefore be made clear


what the main sources of funding are, with details about revenues and expenses,


especially when the reader is a primary part of resource raising (through subscriptions or crowdfunding, for example).

_Agência Pública_ offers visualizations with the main data (**Figure 14**).


**Figure 14 –** _**Agência Pública**_ **presents graphs on expenses and revenues**


Source: _Agência Pública_. Screenshot taken on Jul. 18, 2021.


Founded in 2011, _Agência Pública_, a non-profit digital investigative journalism enterprise that has a section dedicated to the use of data in journalism, makes available on its transparency page [114] the names of the main sponsoring entities, as well as sources of funding over the course of a year (**Figure 14**) and the rules [115] for those wishing to provide support, ensuring that there is no interference in journalistic publications and that sources cannot read content in advance before an article or investigation is published.


Another journalistic organization that prioritizes the disclosure of revenues and expenses in


a section of its website, titled _prestação de contas_ [accountability], is the _Fiquem Sabendo_ agency, specialized in the Freedom of Information Act.


114 Available at: https://apublica.org/transparencia/
115 Available at: https://apublica.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/06/politica-financiamento-publica2021.pdf
116 Available at: https://fiquemsabendo.com.br/prestacao-de-contas/


**Figure 15 –** _**Fiquem Sabendo**_ **has a specific accountability section**


Source: _Fiquem Sabendo_. Screenshot taken on Aug. 16, 2021.


In addition to showing readers a table with information about annual percentages and revenues, _Fiquem Sabendo_ also gathers other documentation in its


accountability section, such as financial statements and balance sheets. Another relevant detail is the nominal identification of its main individual funders,


as well as a display with a list of all supporters.


Having mentioned these examples, it is still necessary to note that every journalistic outlet will disclose only what it considers necessary and prudent, producing the


transparency effect we discussed earlier. This type of conduct — opening up finances with a certain level of detail — tends to be more common among digital natives, which normally depend on donations from large investors, non-profit organizations, or subscriptions to survive.


**5.2.1.5 Editorial guidelines and editorial principles**


Details about the reporter's conduct in their fact-gathering processes, as well as


information about the mission of a journalistic company and its hierarchy, alongside technical and writing standards, are typically present in the editorial guidelines and/or in a


space dedicated to the editorial policy of the newspaper. Through this material, the reader becomes


capable of understanding journalistic practice that goes beyond stylistic matters, accentuated in the realm of editorial decisions.


Thus, the editorial guidelines serve as a reference to which users and subscribers


can turn when they suspect that the newspaper is not fulfilling the basic premises


of journalism. This material normally includes, for example, the journalists' code of conduct. The digital newspaper _Nexo_, which has a close relationship with its readers, including


through closed groups on digital social networks, makes available on its page the _Política Editorial_ [Editorial Policy] [117]:


**Figure 16 –** _**Nexo**_ **presents its editorial policy to readers**


Source: _Nexo Jornal_. Screenshot taken on Jul. 18, 2021.


117 Available at: https://www.nexojornal.com.br/sobre/Padr%C3%B5es-editoriais-do-Nexo


The editorial standards advocated by _Nexo_ (**Figure 16**) provide information about


the choice of topics that become news items and how the sources related to that topic are selected. It also reveals the criteria taken into account for the use of


anonymous sources and the "other side," when the information published requires an official


statement.


Finally, the editorial policy presented by _Nexo_ also engages with ethical issues and the diversity policy proposed by the newsroom. Founded in November 2015,


_Nexo_ holds membership [118] in the _Projeto Credibilidade_ [Trust Project], as do other newspapers mentioned in this thesis, which presupposes quality standards to be fulfilled in the name of accuracy of information.


**5.2.1.6 Diversity policy**


Ensuring the existence of different topics of public interest is one of the commitments


of journalism and, in order to fulfill it, this premise must be foreseen and exposed among


the purposes of a journalistic company. To this end, there is an observed need to


hire professionals with different backgrounds and experiences, so that a variety of topics for coverage and debate can be managed.


At _Folha de S.Paulo_, for example, the diversity policy is located among the


conduct provisions of the _Manual da Redação_ [Editorial Guidelines] [119].


**Figure 17 – Diversity policy is presented at** _**Folha**_


Source: _Folha de S. Paulo_. Screenshot taken on Jul. 22, 2021.


118 Available at: https://www.nexojornal.com.br/sobre/Sobre-o-Nexo
119 Available at: https://temas.folha.uol.com.br/folha-projeto-editorial/manual-de-redacao-conduta/politica-de-diversidade.shtml


**Figure 17** shows that, in its statement, _Folha_ reinforces the importance of


implementing a diversity policy to ensure the plurality of experiences of its


journalists, allowing for new perspectives on topics of journalistic coverage. In practical terms, the newspaper created a specific role: in May 2021, Flávia Lima,


the former ombudsman, assumed the position of diversity editor at the newspaper [120].


The section had already come into operation two years earlier to broaden the variety of


professionals working at the newspaper, as well as the sources consulted taking into account racial, gender, and geographic considerations. One of the first measures in accordance with this


policy was the creation of a _trainees_ program cohort at _Folha_ intended exclusively for Black people. The first cohort, whose activities began in May


2021, is made up of 18 members.


One of the limitations inherent to the diversity policy of _Folha_ and other outlets that set out to do so concerns the difficulty of monitoring these actions —


that is, beyond what is openly communicated by the outlet, it is difficult to monitor and enforce compliance with these policies.


120 Available at: https://www1.folha.uol.com.br/poder/2021/05/jornalismo-nao-fala-mais-so-paraclasse-media-branca-diz-nova-editora-de-diversidade-da-folha.shtml


**5.2.1.7 Privacy policy**


The General Personal Data Protection Law (LGPD), approved in August 2018 and


in force in Brazil two years later, ensures, among other aspects, that citizens


receive clear and accessible information about the handling of their personal data.


Since mid-2020, it has been common for companies that require any type of


registration to make available information about the adjustments and uses of Brazilians' personal data.


In journalism, this also applies to digital footprints and registrations


in _newsletter_ services, for example. The journalistic site _Núcleo_ details the type of information collected by the site, making available a list on a specific page [121]


about the privacy of data collected by the site:


**Figure 18 –** _**Núcleo**_ **opens its privacy policy to readers**


Source: _Núcleo Jornalismo de Dados_. Screenshot taken on Jul. 18, 2021.


121 Available at: https://nucleo.jor.br/privacidade


Since digital platforms become a way for outlets to earn money or trade targeted advertising, it becomes essential to clarify what is done with the information collected from users.


Additionally, _Núcleo_ provides data about the audience [122] that accesses the site. The


information comes from _Google Analytics_, a tool used to count access data to a Web page.


**Figure 19 –** _**Núcleo**_ **presents traffic data from its site**


Source: _Núcleo Jornalismo de Dados_. Screenshot taken on Jul. 18, 2021.


In this case, _Núcleo_'s effort to appear transparent to its audience is evident.

For competitive reasons, the majority of media outlets do not disclose


this type of information. At the end of 2021, the newspaper also began to publish some demographic data, as well as the main sites and social networks through which users access its content.


122 Available at: https://nucleo.jor.br/audiencia


**5.2.1.8 Type of content**


The distinction between informational content (news articles and reports) and opinion content (columns and editorials, for example) is normally made explicit through the use of labels or


other graphic resources that are quite clear on newspaper pages and Web pages.

During the Covid-19 pandemic, some media outlets even began


to make informational content related to the disease available free of charge, given


that it constitutes a universal public health issue. For precisely this reason, it became


even more fundamental to indicate the news coverage and differentiate it from opinion content. During this pandemic period, journalists needed to carefully evaluate the


specialists consulted as sources.

Some media outlets took advantage of the pandemic's circumstances to


create strategies related to media literacy with audiences. A reference press outlet in southern Brazil, the _GaúchaZH_ site created the series _Fluência em Notícias_ [News Literacy]. One of its


publications, released on October 15, 2020, bears the title _Veja como identificar o que é notícia e o que é opinião em GZH_ [See How to Identify What Is News and What Is Opinion at GZH] [123]:


**Figure 20 –** _**GZH**_ **created a series called** _**Fluência em Notícias**_


Source: _GaúchaZH_. Screenshot taken on Jul. 22, 2021.


123 Available at: https://gauchazh.clicrbs.com.br/geral/noticia/2020/10/veja-como-identificar-o-que-e-noticia-e-o-que-eopiniao-em-gzh-ckg9qjytv000d015xwqi0piff.html


As can be seen in **Figure 21**, each topic in the project presents a


brief textual explanation of its proposal and a video that presents, in detail, how


the reader gains autonomy to identify the differences between information and opinion. The series


_Fluência em Notícias_ functions as a media literacy initiative and also


presents topics such as combating disinformation, story suggestions, and information about how a news story is produced in the newsroom. It thus acknowledges that the opening of part of the editorial processes is fundamental for audiences to understand how journalistic products are created.


**5.2.2 Operational level: the method**


At the operational level, transparency appears in the journalistic product as an **operator of an ethical practice** — that is, the degree to which it demonstrates, in its methods and procedures, information about sources consulted, original documents, and permission for


verification and reproducibility of the methodology. In this section, I present seven elements:


**publication date**; **documents**; **data sources**; **degrees of uncertainty**; **reporting markers**;


**methodology**; and **reproducibility and open source**.


**5.2.2.1 Publication date**


The publication date of content is an indispensable element, as it encompasses


the idea of currency and of a verifiable truth, subject to constant updates.


Although it may seem obvious, the publication date of a news article or report is not always


clearly displayed on a news portal, or does not appear at the top of the page for quick and immediate identification by readers.

More than just reporting a basic piece of data, the availability of the publication date


is particularly useful for combating disinformation, since one of the main ways


this occurs in the digital environment is through the recirculation of old content. During the Covid-19 pandemic, this becomes even more dangerous, since outdated data


can be circulated out of context.


In **Figure 21**, the _Núcleo_ site, which specializes in Data-Driven Journalism,


presents a highlighted notice, emphasizing that the article in question, entitled _Na verdade, a qualidade da conexão à internet parece ter melhorado na quarentena_ [Actually, Internet Connection Quality Seems to Have Improved During Quarantine] (SPAGNUOLO, 2020), was published a year before the date the reader accessed it.


**Figure 21 – Banner recalling the publication date is highlighted at** _**Núcleo**_


Source: _Núcleo Jornalismo de Dados_. Screenshot taken on May 18, 2021.


The highlights in **Figure 21** show that the original date of a publication can


be emphasized. It also warns the reader to avoid sharing it out of context, preventing


the recirculation of information in a distorted manner. As discussed in


**Chapter 2**, the inclusion of alerts to prevent distorted sharing is an effective


measure against disinformation.


**5.2.2.2 Documents**


The disclosure of documents consulted as a source in an article, via hyperlink


or even in the body of the text — even more important in the case of scientific studies, in which documentation is not always open —, confers a kind of proof of truth


(LISBOA; BENETTI, 2015) regarding the reported content. It implies opening the document in full to the reader, and not just a specific excerpt, as generally occurs.


In the following report, published by _Agência Pública_ under the title _Influenciadores digitais receberam R$ 23 mil do governo Bolsonaro para propagandear "atendimento precoce" contra Covid-19_ [Digital Influencers Received R$ 23,000 from the Bolsonaro Government to Promote "Early Treatment" Against Covid-19] (FLECK; MARTINS, 2021), access to the amount of public money


paid to digital influencers was obtained through the Freedom of Information Act (LAI).

As a complement to the provided list, the publication includes access, via hyperlink, to the original document accompanying the response to the request submitted via LAI.


**Figure 22 – Official letter accompanies report by** _**Agência Pública**_


Source: _Agência Pública_. Screenshot taken on May 18, 2021.


As can be observed in **Figure 22**, the document used as a source is


made available in full for consultation. On the left in the image, the word _ofício_ [official letter] appears


underlined and, by accessing the hyperlink, the reader obtains the publication (on the right in **Figure 22**).


In other words, _Agência Pública_ does not make available only part of the document in the article itself, but allows the reader to consult it in its entirety.


**5.2.2.3 Data sources**


Revealing the information sources used in the preparation of a report is


part of the journalist's ethical conduct. When dealing with a documentary source whose access


by the reader can occur firsthand, such as public databases, there is


the possibility of sharing, which generally occurs through the availability of original sources via hyperlink.

In the text published by _El País_ Brasil under the title _Mortes entre caixas, frentistas e motoristas de ônibus aumentaram 60% no Brasil no auge da pandemia_ [Deaths Among Cashiers, Gas Station Attendants, and Bus Drivers Increased 60% in Brazil at the Height of the Pandemic] (SOARES, 2021), two situations related to the use and availability of data


sources can be observed. The first, as can be seen in **Figure 23**, concerns the availability of an external hyperlink directed at the original source.


**Figure 23 – Hyperlinks refer to data sources in** _**El País**_


Source: The author (2021). Screenshot taken on May 18, 2021.
